Abu Hashim's Claim: The First Obligation is Doubt (First Refutation)
Al-Taftazani presents Abu Hashim's claim and the first refutation with an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's claim that doubt is the first obligation and the first refutation based on doubt not being within human power.

Core Idea
Doubt is not a commanded act; it is a passive state, not a chosen action.

Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazili, argued that before one can reflect, one must first doubt. But is doubt something we can be commanded to do? The first refutation challenges the very premise that doubt is within our control. This lesson introduces the claim and its initial critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: claim, rationale, then refutation with objection and response.
Pay attention to the term 'al-shakk' (doubt) and how it is defined later.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's claim and its justification
Find the sentence stating that the first obligation is doubt. Understand his reasoning: doubt is necessary to set the intention to reflect, because reflection requires having the two sides of a proposition in mind without believing either.
This is based on the idea that you cannot seek something you already know.
3
Analyze the first refutation
The first refutation argues that doubt is not within power (ghayr maqdur) because it is a quality (kayfiyya) like knowledge. Only its acquisition or continuation is within power, but neither is a prerequisite for the absolute obligation.
Contrast this with acts like prayer, which are directly chosen.
4
Understand the objection from al-Mawaqif and its dismissal
The objection claimed that if doubt were not in power, then knowledge would also not be in power since they are opposites. The dismissal states that knowledge itself is not in power either; only its acquisition is, so the objection fails.
This shows the consistency of the refuter's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو هاشم أول الواجبات هو الشك (٢) لتوقف القصد إلى النظر عليه (۳) إذ لا بد من فهم الطرفين والنسبة مع عدم اعتقاد المطلوب أو نقيضه على ما سبق ورد بوجهين .
أحدهما : أن الشك ليس بمقدور لكونه من الكيفيات كالعلم وإنما المقدور تحصيله أو استدامته بأن يحصل تصور الطرفين ويترك النظر في النسبة ولا شيء منهما بمقدمة، واعتراض المواقف (٤) بأنه لو لم يكن مقدوراً لم يكن العلم مقدوراً لأنه ضده ونسبة القدرة إلى الضدين على السواء ساقط، بما اعترف به من أن العلم ليس بمقدور، وإنما المقدور تحصيله بمباشرة الأسباب .
وثانيهما : أن وجوب النظر والمعرفة مقيد بالشك، لما سبق من أنه لا إمكان للنظر بدونه فضلا عن الوجوب، فهو لا يكون مقدمة للواجب المطلق، بل للمقيد به كالنصاب للزكاة والاستطاعة للحج، فلا يجب تحصيله، ولما كان (۱) إيجاب المعرفة هو إيجاب النظر . قال في المواقف : إن وجوب المعرفة مقيد بالشك، وإلا فالقول بوجوب الشك إنما يبنى (۲) على كونه مقدمة للنظر، لا للمعرفة وكلا الوجهين ضعيف .
أما الأول : فلأنهم لا يعنون بمقدورية مقدمة الواجب أن يكون من الأفعال الاختيارية، بل أن يتمكن المكلف من تحصيله كالطهارة للصلاة وملك النصاب للزكاة، ومعنى وجوبها، وجوب تحصيلها.
وأما الثاني : فلأنه يقتضي أن لا يجب النظر والمعرفة عند الوهم، أو الظن، أو التقليد أو الجهل المركب وفساده بين ويمكن دفع الوهم، والظن بأن الشك الشك يتناولهما لأن معناه التردد في النسبة (۳) إما على استواء وهـو المحض أو رجحان لأحد الجانبين وهو الظن والوهم، ودفع التقليد، والجهل المركب بأن الواجب معها هو النظر في الدليل، ومعرفة وجـه دلالته ليؤلا إلى العلم ، وذلك لأن امتناع النظر والطلب عند الجزم بالمطلوب أو نقيضه مما لم يقع فيه نزاع، وقد يقال في رد الشك المحض (٤) أنه وإن كان مقدمة للنظر الواجب فليس من أسبابه ليكون إيجابه إيجاباً له بمعنى تعلق خطاب الشارع به وفيه نظر، لأن مراد أبي هاشم هو الوجوب العقلي كالنظر والمعرفة . نعم لو قيل : إنه ليس من المعاني التي يطلبها العاقل، ويحكم باستحقاق تاركه الذم لكان شيئاً . وستعرف فساد النظر بمعرفة معنى الوجوب العقلي (٥) .
Abu Hashim said: The first obligation is doubt (al-shakk), because the intention to reflect depends on it, since one must understand the two sides and the relation without belief in the sought or its opposite, as previously mentioned. It is refuted in two aspects. The first: that doubt is not within power because it is among the qualities like knowledge; rather, what is within power is its acquisition or its continuation, that is, by obtaining the conception of the two sides and refraining from reflection on the relation, and neither of these is a prerequisite. And the objection of al-Mawaqif that if it were not within power, then knowledge would not be within power because it (doubt) is its opposite, and the relation of power to opposites is equal, is fallen, given the admission that knowledge is not within power; rather, what is within power is its acquisition by directly engaging the causes. The second: that the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, because as previously mentioned, there is no possibility of reflection without it, let alone obligation. Thus it (doubt) is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligation, but rather for that which is conditioned by it, like the nisab for zakat and the ability (istita'a) for hajj; therefore its acquisition is not obligatory. And since making knowledge obligatory is making reflection obligatory, he said in al-Mawaqif: 'The obligation of knowledge is conditioned by doubt; otherwise, the claim that doubt is obligatory is based on it being a prerequisite for reflection, not for knowledge, and both aspects are weak.' As for the first: because they (the opponents) do not mean by 'being within power' of a prerequisite that it be among voluntary acts, but rather that the legally responsible person is able to acquire it, like purification for prayer and possession of the nisab for zakat, and the meaning of its obligation is the obligation to acquire it. As for the second: because it requires that reflection and knowledge would not be obligatory in cases of wahm (conjecture), zann (opinion), taqlid (blind following), or jahl murakkab (compounded ignorance), and its corruption is evident. And one can repel wahm and zann by saying that doubt encompasses them, because its meaning is hesitation regarding the relation, either equally—which is pure doubt (al-shakk al-mahd)—or with preponderance of one side, which is zann and wahm. And one can repel taqlid and jahl murakkab by saying that what is obligatory along with them is reflection on the evidence and knowledge of the aspect of its indication, so that it leads to knowledge. This is because the impossibility of reflection and seeking when one is certain of the sought or its opposite is not disputed. And it may be said in refutation of pure doubt that even if it is a prerequisite for obligatory reflection, it is not among its causes, such that making it obligatory would be making reflection obligatory in the sense of the address of the Lawgiver attaching to it. But there is consideration in this, because Abu Hashim intended rational obligation (al-wujub al-'aqli) like reflection and knowledge. Yes, if it were said that it is not among the meanings that a rational person seeks and judges the one who abandons it to be blameworthy, that would be something. And you will come to know the corruption of reflection by knowing the meaning of rational obligation.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i's claim that the first obligation is doubt (al-shakk), because the intention to reflect depends on doubt—one must understand the two sides of the proposition and the relation between them, without holding belief in the sought or its opposite. The author then gives two refutations of this view. The first refutation: doubt is not within human power (ghayr maqdur) because it is a quality (kayfiyya) like knowledge; only the acquisition or continuation of doubt is within power, but neither acquisition nor continuation is a prerequisite (muqaddima) for the absolute obligation. The author notes an objection from al-Mawaqif that if doubt were not within power, then knowledge also would not be within power since they are opposites, but this objection is dismissed because it is admitted that knowledge itself is not within power—only its acquisition is. The second refutation: the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, so doubt is a prerequisite for a conditioned obligation, not for the absolute obligation, similar to the nisab for zakat or ability (istita'a) for hajj; thus acquiring doubt is not obligatory. The author then quotes al-Mawaqif's response that both refutations are weak. He explains why the first is weak: the opponents (refuters) do not mean by 'being within power' that the prerequisite must be a voluntary act, but rather that the mukallaf is able to acquire it, like purification for prayer (which may not be voluntary in every detail but is still acquirable). The second refutation is weak because it would imply that reflection and knowledge are not obligatory in cases of wahm, zann, taqlid, or jahl murakkab, which is false. Wahm and zann are included under doubt because the meaning of shakk is hesitation (taraddud) in the relation, either equal (pure doubt) or with preponderance (zann/wahm). Taqlid and jahl murakkab are addressed by saying that the obligation in those states is to reflect on evidence to reach knowledge, since reflection is impossible when one is certain. Finally, a further point: even if pure doubt is a prerequisite for reflection, it is not a cause, so making it obligatory would not directly oblige reflection—but this is considered, because Abu Hashim means rational obligation (wujub 'aqli), not legal obligation. The passage ends with a note that the corruption of this view will be understood later.

Key Terms
الشك
النظر
المعرفة
المقدورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That doubt is a commanded act like prayer or fasting.
Correction: The passage clarifies that doubt is a passive quality, not a voluntary action, so it cannot be directly obligated.

Checkpoint
According to the first refutation, why is doubt not considered to be within human power?
A) because it is a quality like knowledge
B) because it is a voluntary act
C) because it leads to knowledge
D) because it is required for reflection

Key Takeaway
Doubt is not a commanded act; it is a passive state that one cannot be directly obligated to produce.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from the duty to seek knowledge of Allah by saying you are in a state of doubt? This lesson shows that doubt is not a commanded state—it is not even within your power. What concrete action will you take this week to begin reflecting on the evidence for His existence, despite your feelings?
?
The refutation concedes that knowledge itself is not directly under your control; only its acquisition through means is. When you pray for knowledge, do you also take the means—reading, studying, asking scholars? What means will you commit to starting tomorrow, without waiting for a perfect state of certainty?
?
When you face an argument in aqeedah, do you use doubt as a shield to avoid intellectual responsibility? The passage teaches that doubt is a passive quality, not an active command. Are you using doubt to procrastinate on your obligation to reflect? Decide now: what is the first step you will take to engage with the proofs of tawheed?

Method of Learning
intermediate
7 min
The Second Refutation and Its Critique
Al-Taftazani presents the second refutation and the rebuttals from al-Mawaqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second refutation of Abu Hashim's claim and the counter-arguments that show its weakness.

Core Idea
Obligations conditioned on doubt make doubt a prerequisite, not an absolute duty.

The second refutation argues that even if doubt is necessary for reflection, it is only a condition for a conditioned obligation, not an absolute one. But critics respond that this would exempt many from the duty to know Allah. This lesson explores the back-and-forth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the second refutation
Locate the second refutation: it states that the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, so doubt is like the nisab for zakat—a prerequisite, not itself obligatory.
Note the analogy with zakat and hajj.
2
Understand the response from al-Mawaqif
Al-Mawaqif responds that both refutations are weak. The passage then gives two reasons why the first refutation is weak: they misunderstand 'being within power' as requiring voluntary action, when it only means being able to acquire it.
Example: purification for prayer may not be voluntary in detail, but it is acquirable.
3
Examine the critique of the second refutation
The second refutation is weak because it would imply that reflection and knowledge are not obligatory in cases of conjecture, opinion, blind following, or compounded ignorance, which is false.
This shows the refutation leads to unacceptable consequences.

وقال أبو هاشم أول الواجبات هو الشك (٢) لتوقف القصد إلى النظر عليه (۳) إذ لا بد من فهم الطرفين والنسبة مع عدم اعتقاد المطلوب أو نقيضه على ما سبق ورد بوجهين .
أحدهما : أن الشك ليس بمقدور لكونه من الكيفيات كالعلم وإنما المقدور تحصيله أو استدامته بأن يحصل تصور الطرفين ويترك النظر في النسبة ولا شيء منهما بمقدمة، واعتراض المواقف (٤) بأنه لو لم يكن مقدوراً لم يكن العلم مقدوراً لأنه ضده ونسبة القدرة إلى الضدين على السواء ساقط، بما اعترف به من أن العلم ليس بمقدور، وإنما المقدور تحصيله بمباشرة الأسباب .

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prerequisites like doubt must be voluntary acts to be obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the rebuttal of the second refutation, what would be the consequence if the second refutation were accepted?
A) Reflection would become impossible
B) Knowledge would be unconditionally obligatory
C) Reflection and knowledge would not be obligatory in cases of conjecture, opinion, blind following, or compounded ignorance
D) Doubt would become a voluntary act

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you currently in a state of doubt about fundamental beliefs? If so, are you actively reflecting to resolve it, or are you content to remain in uncertainty? What would change if you took the obligation of reflection seriously starting today?
?
The passage warns that even those with compounded ignorance are obliged to reflect. Is there any area of your deen where you have blindly followed without understanding, assuming it suffices? How can you begin to replace blind following with genuine knowledge this week?
?
Many people assume they are exempt from seeking deeper knowledge because they already hold some belief (even if incorrect). In what specific area of your aqeedah have you become complacent, thinking your current knowledge is sufficient? What concrete step can you take to challenge that comfort tomorrow?

Addressing Objections: Wahm, Zann, Taqlid, and the Nature of Doubt
Al-Taftazani explains how to address wahm, zann, taqlid, and jahl murakkab, and clarifies the nature of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the discussion handles cases of conjecture, opinion, blind following, and compounded ignorance, and the final nuance about rational obligation.

Core Idea
Even in non-doubt states, the duty to reflect remains, and the obligation is rational, not legal.

The earlier critique of the second refutation raised a problem: what about people who have some belief but not certainty? This lesson shows how the category of doubt can be extended and clarifies that the obligation in such cases is still to reflect. It concludes with a key distinction between rational and legal obligation.

Guided Path
1
See how doubt is extended to wahm and zann
The passage explains that doubt (shakk) in its broader sense includes wahm and zann because they involve hesitation (taraddud) in the relation, just with preponderance.
Pure doubt is equal hesitation; wahm and zann have imbalance.
2
Understand the handling of taqlid and jahl murakkab
For blind followers and those with compounded ignorance, the obligation is not to doubt but to reflect on evidence to attain knowledge.
Reflection is possible here because certainty is absent.
3
Grasp the final point about pure doubt and rational obligation
Even if pure doubt is a prerequisite for reflection, it is not a cause, so obliging it does not directly oblige reflection. However, Abu Hashim's claim was about rational obligation, not legal.
Rational obligation is based on what a rational person would seek, not on divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Abu Hashim said: The first obligation is doubt (al-shakk), because the intention to reflect depends on it, since one must understand the two sides and the relation without belief in the sought or its opposite, as previously mentioned. It is refuted in two aspects. The first: that doubt is not within power because it is among the qualities like knowledge; rather, what is within power is its acquisition or its continuation, that is, by obtaining the conception of the two sides and refraining from reflection on the relation, and neither of these is a prerequisite. And the objection of al-Mawaqif that if it were not within power, then knowledge would not be within power because it (doubt) is its opposite, and the relation of power to opposites is equal, is fallen, given the admission that knowledge is not within power; rather, what is within power is its acquisition by directly engaging the causes. The second: that the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, because as previously mentioned, there is no possibility of reflection without it, let alone obligation. Thus it (doubt) is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligation, but rather for that which is conditioned by it, like the nisab for zakat and the ability (istita'a) for hajj; therefore its acquisition is not obligatory. And since making knowledge obligatory is making reflection obligatory, he said in al-Mawaqif: 'The obligation of knowledge is conditioned by doubt; otherwise, the claim that doubt is obligatory is based on it being a prerequisite for reflection, not for knowledge, and both aspects are weak.' As for the first: because they (the opponents) do not mean by 'being within power' of a prerequisite that it be among voluntary acts, but rather that the legally responsible person is able to acquire it, like purification for prayer and possession of the nisab for zakat, and the meaning of its obligation is the obligation to acquire it. As for the second: because it requires that reflection and knowledge would not be obligatory in cases of wahm (conjecture), zann (opinion), taqlid (blind following), or jahl murakkab (compounded ignorance), and its corruption is evident. And one can repel wahm and zann by saying that doubt encompasses them, because its meaning is hesitation regarding the relation, either equally—which is pure doubt (al-shakk al-mahd)—or with preponderance of one side, which is zann and wahm. And one can repel taqlid and jahl murakkab by saying that what is obligatory along with them is reflection on the evidence and knowledge of the aspect of its indication, so that it leads to knowledge. This is because the impossibility of reflection and seeking when one is certain of the sought or its opposite is not disputed. And it may be said in refutation of pure doubt that even if it is a prerequisite for obligatory reflection, it is not among its causes, such that making it obligatory would be making reflection obligatory in the sense of the address of the Lawgiver attaching to it. But there is consideration in this, because Abu Hashim intended rational obligation (al-wujub al-'aqli) like reflection and knowledge. Yes, if it were said that it is not among the meanings that a rational person seeks and judges the one who abandons it to be blameworthy, that would be something. And you will come to know the corruption of reflection by knowing the meaning of rational obligation.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i's claim that the first obligation is doubt (al-shakk), because the intention to reflect depends on doubt—one must understand the two sides of the proposition and the relation between them, without holding belief in the sought or its opposite. The author then gives two refutations of this view. The first refutation: doubt is not within human power (ghayr maqdur) because it is a quality (kayfiyya) like knowledge; only the acquisition or continuation of doubt is within power, but neither acquisition nor continuation is a prerequisite (muqaddima) for the absolute obligation. The author notes an objection from al-Mawaqif that if doubt were not within power, then knowledge also would not be within power since they are opposites, but this objection is dismissed because it is admitted that knowledge itself is not within power—only its acquisition is. The second refutation: the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, so doubt is a prerequisite for a conditioned obligation, not for the absolute obligation, similar to the nisab for zakat or ability (istita'a) for hajj; thus acquiring doubt is not obligatory. The author then quotes al-Mawaqif's response that both refutations are weak. He explains why the first is weak: the opponents (refuters) do not mean by 'being within power' that the prerequisite must be a voluntary act, but rather that the mukallaf is able to acquire it, like purification for prayer (which may not be voluntary in every detail but is still acquirable). The second refutation is weak because it would imply that reflection and knowledge are not obligatory in cases of wahm, zann, taqlid, or jahl murakkab, which is false. Wahm and zann are included under doubt because the meaning of shakk is hesitation (taraddud) in the relation, either equal (pure doubt) or with preponderance (zann/wahm). Taqlid and jahl murakkab are addressed by saying that the obligation in those states is to reflect on evidence to reach knowledge, since reflection is impossible when one is certain. Finally, a further point: even if pure doubt is a prerequisite for reflection, it is not a cause, so making it obligatory would not directly oblige reflection—but this is considered, because Abu Hashim means rational obligation (wujub 'aqli), not legal obligation. The passage ends with a note that the corruption of this view will be understood later.

Key Terms
الوهم
الظن
التقليد
الجهل المركب
الشك المحض
الوجوب العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That blind followers are exempt from the duty to know Allah.
Correction: The text states that the duty for them is to reflect on evidence, not to doubt.

Checkpoint
How does the passage address the issue of blind following (taqlid) with regard to the obligation of reflection?
A) It says blind followers are excused from reflection
B) It says the obligation for them is to reflect on evidence
C) It says blind followers must first doubt
D) It says blind followers are equivalent to pure doubt

Key Takeaway
Even in non-doubt states, the duty to seek knowledge remains; the obligation is rational, not legal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you hold a belief simply because you trust a scholar or a book, without truly understanding the evidence yourself, are you fulfilling the rational obligation that this lesson demands? What would it cost you to actually reflect on that belief today?
?
If obligation is rational rather than legal, then your own mind holds you accountable. Do you truly seek the truth with your intellect, or do you stop at comfort and certainty? What would it take for you to embrace the discomfort of doubt as a path to knowledge?
?
The passage says that even pure doubt does not exempt you from reflecting. Have you ever used uncertainty as an excuse to avoid seeking truth? Will you now take one concrete step to reflect on a matter you have been avoiding?

Classification of Evidence: Rational and Transmitted
Classifies dalil into rational and transmitted, and discusses the problem of purely transmitted proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why all evidence ultimately relies on reason, even when it appears to be purely transmitted.

Core Idea
No evidence can be purely transmitted; every proof rests on a rational foundation.

Sanusi introduces the foundational classification of evidence into rational and transmitted. This lesson examines why even transmitted evidence cannot stand without reason, and how the method of proof changes depending on the nature of the claim. The passage clarifies a key principle: all theological argumentation ultimately depends on rational certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi defines the two main types of evidence and immediately addresses the problem of pure transmitted evidence.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two categories and their criterion
Find the criterion for rational vs. transmitted evidence. Understand what 'depends on transmission' means.
The criterion is whether the evidence relies on any naql (report) at all.
3
Understand why pure transmitted evidence is invalid
The passage states that pure transmitted evidence is bāṭil (invalid). Look for the reason: the truthfulness of the transmitter must be established by reason.
This is a key point: reason is always required at some level.
4
Examine the three cases for proof method
The passage ends with a rule for how to prove a judgment depending on its relation to reason and transmission. Identify the three scenarios described.
These scenarios reflect the interplay between reason and transmission.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this classification matters for theology. How does it affect arguments using scripture?
All transmitted evidence (like Quran, hadith) ultimately requires rational verification of the transmitter's reliability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال) : والدليل إن لم يتوقف على نقل أصلاً فعقلي وإلا فنقلي، سواء توقف كل من مقدماته القريبة على النقل أو لا وقد يخص النقل الأول ويسمى الثاني مركباً، أما النقلي المحض فباطل إذ لا بد من ثبوت صدق المخير بالعقل، والمطلوب إن استوى طرفاه عند العقل فإثباته بالنقل، وإلا فإن توقف ثبوت النقل عليه فبالعقل وإلا فبكل منها.
(He said): The evidence (al-dalīl): if it does not depend on transmission (naql) at all, then it is rational ('aqlī); otherwise it is transmitted (naqlī), whether each of its proximate premises depends on transmission or not. Sometimes the first is specified as transmitted and the second is called composite (murakkab). As for pure transmitted evidence, it is invalid, for the truthfulness of the transmitter (muḵbir) must be established by reason. And the requested [judgment] – if its two sides are equal in the view of reason, then its proof is by transmission; otherwise, if the proof of the transmission depends on it, then [it is proved] by reason; otherwise, by both.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of evidence (dalīl) into two main types: rational (‘aqlī) and transmitted (naqlī). The criterion is whether the evidence depends on any transmission (naql), i.e., on reports from a trustworthy source. If it does not depend on transmission at all, it is rational; otherwise it is transmitted. The author notes that within transmitted evidence, sometimes the term 'transmitted' is reserved for the first type (where all premises are transmitted), and the second type (where some premises are transmitted and some rational) is called 'composite' (murakkab). However, the key objection is that pure transmitted evidence is impossible (bāṭil) because the truthfulness of the transmitter must be established by reason. Without a rational basis to trust the transmitter, the transmitted evidence lacks foundation. The author then explains how the method of proof depends on the nature of the requested judgment: if reason sees both sides as equally possible (e.g., a non-necessary judgment), then transmission is needed; if the judgment is something on which the proof of the transmission itself depends (e.g., the truthfulness of the transmitter), then reason must be used; otherwise, both reason and transmission may be used together.

Key Terms
عقلي ('aqlī)
نقلي (naqlī)
مركب (murakkab)
نقل (naql)
مقدمات قريبة (muqaddimāt qarība)
صدق المخبر (ṣidq al-muḵbir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transmitted proof (like scripture) can stand alone without any rational support.
Correction: The truthfulness of the transmitter must be rationally established; thus all transmitted proofs ultimately rely on reason.

Checkpoint
Why is pure transmitted evidence considered invalid according to the passage?
A) Because it contains too many premises.
B) Because it does not rely on reason at all.
C) Because the truthfulness of the transmitter must be established by reason, so pure transmitted evidence is impossible.
D) Because transmission is always uncertain.

Key Takeaway
All evidence ultimately depends on reason; transmitted evidence is never purely independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious belief or practice solely because 'the scholars said so' without rationally verifying the basis of that authority? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the rational foundation of your most deeply held beliefs?
?
When you read the Quran or Hadith, do you always consciously acknowledge that your acceptance of their truth depends on a rational judgment about the trustworthiness of the transmission? How might this awareness change the way you approach divine revelation?
?
The author says pure transmitted evidence is impossible. How does this confront your tendency to rely on blind imitation (taqlid) in matters of faith? What is one specific area of your belief where you need to strengthen the rational foundation?

The Threefold Division of Evidence and the Role of Premises
Al-Taftazani explains the threefold division and clarifies the role of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of evidence (rational, transmitted, composite) and how they are distinguished by their proximate and remote premises.

Core Idea
Evidence is classified by its premises; even transmitted evidence rests on rational foundations.

This passage from al-Maqasid clarifies a common misunderstanding about the division of evidence. Al-Taftazani explains that the twofold division (rational/transmitted) and the threefold division (adding composite) are not contradictory but based on which premises are considered. He also provides a rule for when reason or transmission alone suffices.

Guided Path
1
Understand the twofold division problem
Notice that the twofold division might imply transmitted evidence has no rational premises, which is false. Read how the author repels this false implication.
2
Learn the threefold distinction
Identify the three types: rational, transmitted, and composite. See the examples given for the transmitted (pilgrimage) and composite (ablution).
The composite type has a mix of rational and transmitted proximate premises.
3
Grasp the role of proximate vs remote premises
Understand why the author restricts to proximate premises: even transmitted evidence has rational remote premises (e.g., verifying truthfulness of reporters).
This avoids the false dichotomy.
4
Apply the rule for classification
Follow the rule: if entailment is by reason's decree, it's rational; otherwise transmitted. Then consider whether reason finds both sides equal, or if transmission depends on reason, to determine if transmission alone, reason alone, or both can prove.
This rule prevents circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يقسم إلى العقلي والنقلي، وقد يقسم إليهما وإلى المركب من العقلي والنقلي، وهذا يوهم أن المراد بالنقلي ما لا يكون شيء من مقدماته عقلياً وهو باطل، إذ لو لم تنته سلسلة صدق المخبرين إلى من يعلم صدقه بالعقل لزم الدور أو التسلسل، فدفع ذلك بأن من حصره فيهما أراد بالنقلي ما يتوقف شيء من مقدماته القريبة أو البعيدة على النقل والسماع من الصادق، وبالعقلي ما لا يكون كذلك، ومن ثلث القسمة أراد بالنقلي ما يكون جميع مقدماته القريبة نقلية كقولنا : الحج واجب وكل واجب فتاركه يستحق العقاب، وبالمركب ما يكون بعض مقدماته القريبة عملياً وبعضها نقلياً كقولنا : الوضوء عمل، وكل عمل فصحته الشرعية بالنية وكقولنا : الحج واجب وكل واجب فتاركه عاص، إذ لا معنى للعصيان إلا ترك امتثال الأوامر والنواهي وإنما قيد المقدمات بالقريبة لأن النقلي أيضاً بعض مقدماته البعيدة عقلية كما مر فلا يقابل المركب بل يندرج فيه هذا إن أريد بالدليل نفس المقدمات المرتبة، وأما إذا أريد مأخذها كالعالم للصانع والكتاب والسنة والإجماع للأحكام، فلا معنی للمركب، وطريق القسمة أن استلزامه للمطلوب إن كان بحكم العقل فعقلي، وإلا فنقلي ثم الحكم المطلوب إن استوى فيه عند العقل جانب الثبوت والانتماء بحيث لا يجد من نفسه سبيلا إلى تعيين أحدهما فطريق إثباته النقل لا غير، كالحكم بوجوب الحج، ويكون زيده في الدار، وإلا فإن توقف عليه ثبوت النقل كالعلم بصدق المخبر، وما يبتنى عليه ذلك، كثبوت الصانع وبعثة النبي ودلالة المعجزة، ونحو ذلك فطريق إثباته العقل لا غير، لئلا يلزم الدور، وإلا فيمكن إثباته بكل من النقل والعقل كوحدة الصانع، وحدوث العالم، إذا صح الاستدلال على الصانع بإمكان العالم أو بحدوث الأعراض، أو بعض الجواهر،
It may be divided into rational and transmitted, and it may be divided into these two and the composite of rational and transmitted. This suggests that the intended meaning of 'transmitted' is that none of its premises are rational, which is false. For if the chain of truthfulness of the reporters does not end with someone whose truthfulness is known by reason, circularity or infinite regress would follow. This is repelled by [saying] that he who restricts it to two types means by 'transmitted' that which depends on transmission and hearing from the truthful in some of its proximate or remote premises, and by 'rational' that which is not so. And he who makes a threefold division means by 'transmitted' that all of its proximate premises are transmitted, such as our saying: 'Pilgrimage is obligatory, and everything obligatory – its abandoner deserves punishment'; and by 'composite' that some of its proximate premises are rational and some are transmitted, such as our saying: 'Ablution is an act, and every act – its legal validity is by intention'; and our saying: 'Pilgrimage is obligatory, and everything obligatory – its abandoner is disobedient', for there is no meaning of disobedience except abandoning compliance with commands and prohibitions. He only restricted it to proximate premises because transmitted [evidence] also has some remote premises that are rational, as previously mentioned, so it does not contrast with composite but rather falls under it. This is if what is meant by 'evidence' is the arranged premises themselves. But if what is meant is their sources – such as the world for the Maker, or the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus for rulings – then there is no meaning for composite. And the way of division is: if its entailment of the required [judgment] is by the judgment of reason, then it is rational; otherwise, transmitted. Then, the required judgment: if in reason its two sides of affirmation and negation are equal, such that it finds no way by itself to determine one of them, then its method of proof is transmission alone, like the judgment of the obligation of pilgrimage, or 'Zayd is in the house'. Otherwise, if the proof of the transmission depends on it – like knowledge of the truthfulness of the reporter and what is based on that, such as the existence of the Maker, the sending of the Prophet, and the indication of the miracle, and the like – then its method of proof is reason alone, to avoid circularity. Otherwise, it can be proved by both transmission and reason, such as the oneness of the Maker and the temporality of the world, if the inference to the Maker is valid by the possibility of the world or by the temporality of accidents or some substances.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of evidence into three types: rational, transmitted, and composite. It begins by noting a potential misunderstanding: the twofold division (rational/transmitted) might be taken to mean that transmitted evidence has no rational premises, which is false because the truthfulness of reporters ultimately rests on rational knowledge. The author clarifies that those who use a twofold division mean by 'transmitted' that some of its proximate or remote premises depend on transmission, and by 'rational' that none do. Those who use a threefold division mean by 'transmitted' that all proximate premises are transmitted (e.g., 'Pilgrimage is obligatory; everything obligatory is punishable for abandonment'), and by 'composite' that some proximate premises are rational and others transmitted (e.g., 'Ablution is an act; every act requires intention for legal validity'). The restriction to proximate premises is necessary because even transmitted evidence has remote rational premises. If by 'evidence' we mean the arranged premises themselves, the composite type is a subset of transmitted. But if by 'evidence' we mean the source (like the world for the Maker, or the Book, Sunnah, and consensus for rulings), then the composite type has no meaning. The passage then gives a rule for classification: if the entailment of the judgment is by reason's decree, the evidence is rational; otherwise it is transmitted. Furthermore, if reason finds both affirmation and negation equally possible, then only transmission can determine which is true (e.g., obligation of pilgrimage). If the proof of transmission itself depends on that judgment (e.g., the truthfulness of the reporter), then only reason can prove it, to avoid circularity. If neither condition applies, both reason and transmission can be used (e.g., oneness of the Maker, temporality of the world).

Key Terms
عقلي ('aqlī)
نقلي (naqlī)
مركب (murakkab)
مقدمات قريبة (muqaddimāt qarība)
مقدمات بعيدة (muqaddimāt ba‘īda)
دليل (dalīl)
مأخذ (ma’khadh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transmitted evidence has no rational premises at all.
Correction: Even transmitted evidence depends on rational premises at the remote level, such as verifying the truthfulness of reporters.

Checkpoint
According to the threefold division, what is an example of a composite (murakkab) evidence?
A) The world is possible, therefore it has a maker.
B) Ablution is an act; every act requires intention for legal validity.
C) Pilgrimage is obligatory; everything obligatory is punishable for abandonment.
D) The Prophet's miracle proves his truthfulness.

Key Takeaway
The threefold division of evidence (rational, transmitted, composite) is distinguished by the nature of its premises, and even transmitted evidence ultimately depends on rational premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear or read Islamic teachings from scholars or books, do you ever critically examine the rational basis for trusting those sources? If not, what keeps you from doing so? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to verify the reliability of a religious claim you accept.
?
This passage shows that even transmitted knowledge ultimately rests on rational premises like the existence of God and the truthfulness of prophets. Have you personally reflected on why you believe in God and prophethood? Are you content with mere tradition, or have you sought rational conviction? Commit to studying one rational proof for God's existence until you can explain it clearly.
?
The text warns that without rational verification, we fall into circularity or infinite regress. In your own life, are there religious beliefs you hold simply because 'my elders said so' without any rational inquiry? Name one such belief and decide today to examine its rational foundation.

Probable vs Certain: The Scope of Transmitted Evidence
Al-Taftazani distinguishes probable from certain transmission and lists basic conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when transmitted evidence yields certainty versus mere probability, and the basic conditions required for certainty.

Core Idea
Certainty from transmitted evidence is conditional; probability comes easily.

Al-Taftazani begins by clarifying that transmitted evidence does not automatically produce certainty. Its dependence on rational proofs applies only in specific contexts. He then outlines the conditions under which transmission can yield certainty, including the role of the transmitter's infallibility and the absence of semantic ambiguities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on transmission scope
Read the Arabic passage once, noting how the author distinguishes between contexts requiring rational proofs and those that do not.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إنما هو في الأحكام الشرعية'.
2
Identify the distinction between probability and certainty
Find where the author says transmission provides probability (ẓann) and where it provides certainty (yaqīn). Note the conditions listed for certainty.
The conditions include 'العام بالوضع والإرادة' and 'عصمة رواة العربية'.
3
Note the five obstacles to certainty
The author lists five things that prevent certainty: naql, ishtirāk, majāz, iḍmār, and muʿāriḍ ʿaqlī. Understand each briefly.
The passage also mentions that qarāʾin (contextual indicators) can remove uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن توقف النقل على ثبوت الصانع، وبعثة الأنبياء، إنما هو في الأحكام الشرعية، وفيما يقصد به حصول القطع وصحة الاحتجاج على الغير، وأما في مجرد إفادة الظن فيكفي خبر واحد أو جماعة يظن المستدل صدقه كالمنقولات عن بعض الأولياء والعلماء أو الشعراء ونحو ذلك، حتى لو جعل العلم الحاصل بالتواتر استدلالياً لم يتوقف النقل القطعي أيضاً على إثبات الصانع، وبعثة الأنبياء. قال : ولا خفاء في إفادة النقل الظن وأما إفادته اليقين فيتوقف على العام بالوضع والإرادة وذلك بعصمة رواة العربية وعدم مثل النقل والاشتراك والمجاز، والإضمار والمعارض من العقلي إذ لا بد معه من تأويل النقل لأنه فرع العقل فتكذيبه تكذيبه نعم قد ينضم إليه قرائن تنفي الاحتمال، فيفيد القطع بالمطلوب، وينفي المعارض في العقبات مثل : قل هو الله أحد، ولا إله إلا الله) . وإنما الكلام في إفادته العلم، فإنها تتوقف على العلم بوضع الألفاظ الواردة في كلام المخبر الصادق للمعاني المفهومة، وبإرادة المخبر تلك المعاني، ليلزم ثبوت المدلول والعلم بالوضع يتوقف على العلم بعصمة رواة العربية، لغة وصرفاً ونحواً عن الغلط والكذب، لأن مرجعه إلى روايتهم إذ لا طريق إلى معرفة الأوضاع سوى النقل، أما الأصول أعني ما وقع التنصيص عليه فظاهر، وأما الفروع فلأنها مبنية على الأصول بالقياس الذي هو في نفسه ظني، والعلم بالإرادة يتوقف على عدم النقل إلى معنى آخر وعلى عدم اشتراكه بين هذا المعنى وبين معنى آخر . = وعلى عدم كونه مستعملا بطريق التجوز في معنى غير الموضوع له، وعلى عدم إضمار شيء يتغير به المعنى، وعلى عدم تخصيص ما ظاهره عموم الأفراد
And know that the dependence of transmission on the establishment of the Maker and the sending of prophets is only in legal rulings and in what is intended to obtain certainty and validity of argument against others. As for merely providing probability, a single report or a group whose truthfulness the arguer thinks likely suffices, like transmitted reports from some saints, scholars, or poets and the like—even if the knowledge obtained by recurrent transmission is considered inferential, then definitive transmission does not depend on proving the Maker and sending of prophets either. He said: There is no obscurity in transmission providing probability; as for its providing certainty, it depends on knowledge of the lexical meaning and intent—that is, on the infallibility of the transmitters of Arabic, and the absence of such things as change of meaning (naql), homonymy (ishtirāk), metaphor (majāz), ellipsis (iḍmār), and a rational counter-evidence (muʿāriḍ ʿaqlī), for it is necessary with it to interpret the transmitted text, because it is subordinate to reason, so to reject it is to reject reason. Indeed, contextual indicators (qarāʾin) may accompany it that remove probability, so it yields certainty about the desired conclusion and removes the counter-evidence in rational matters, such as 'Say: He is God, One' and 'There is no god but God.' The real discussion is about its providing knowledge, for that depends on knowledge of the lexical meanings of the words that appear in the speech of the truthful informant for the understood meanings, and on the informant's intending those meanings, so that the reality of the referent is established. Knowledge of lexical meanings depends on knowledge of the infallibility of the transmitters of Arabic—lexicon, morphology, and syntax—from error and lying, because it relies on their transmission, as there is no way to know lexical meanings except through transmission. As for the fundamentals (uṣūl)—that is, what is explicitly stated—it is clear; as for the branches (furūʿ), they are built on the fundamentals by analogy, which is in itself probable. Knowledge of intent depends on the absence of change (naql) to another meaning, the absence of homonymy (ishtirāk) between this meaning and another, the absence of its being used metaphorically (majāz) for a meaning other than the original, the absence of ellipsis (iḍmār) that changes the meaning, and the absence of specification (takhṣīṣ) of what is apparently general in scope.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage explains when transmitted evidence (naql) yields certainty or mere probability. The author first clarifies that the dependence of transmission on rational proofs for the existence of God and prophethood applies only to legal rulings and when one aims for certainty and valid argumentation. For mere probability, a single report or a group that the arguer thinks truthful suffices. He then quotes an authority (likely the author of the matn) who states that transmission certainly provides probability; for it to provide certainty, it depends on knowledge of the lexical meaning (waḍʿ) and the speaker's intent (irāda). This requires the infallibility (ʿiṣma) of the transmitters of Arabic—i.e., that they are protected from error and lying in transmitting the language (lexicon, morphology, syntax). Additionally, five obstacles must be absent: (1) naql (change of meaning from original to another), (2) ishtirāk (homonymy, where a word has more than one meaning), (3) majāz (metaphorical usage), (4) iḍmār (ellipsis, where something is omitted that changes the meaning), and (5) a countervailing rational proof (muʿāriḍ ʿaqlī), which necessitates interpretation of the transmitted text because reason is foundational. However, contextual indicators (qarāʾin) may remove probability and yield certainty even in the presence of such obstacles, as in clear verses like 'Say: He is God, One.' The core of the discussion is that transmitted evidence produces knowledge (ʿilm) only if one knows the lexical meanings of the words used by the truthful informant and that the informant intended those meanings. Knowledge of lexical meanings depends on the reliability of Arabic transmitters. Fundamentals (uṣūl) explicitly stated are clear; branches (furūʿ) are derived by analogy (qiyās), which is probable. Knowledge of intent requires the absence of the five conditions (naql, ishtirāk, majāz, iḍmār, takhṣīṣ).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that any transmitted report can yield certainty if it is from a reliable source.
Correction: The text shows that certainty requires specific conditions about the language and absence of semantic ambiguities, not just source reliability.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for transmitted evidence to yield certainty?
A) Only that the report is from a reliable source.
B) Knowledge of lexical meanings and intent, plus infallibility of transmitters of Arabic and absence of certain semantic ambiguities.
C) That the report is recurrent (mutawātir).
D) That the report is from a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted evidence yields certainty only when the lexical meaning and the speaker's intent are known, requiring reliable transmission of Arabic and the absence of semantic and rational obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Taftazani says that for certainty, you must know the lexical meaning and the speaker’s intent. How often do you seek to know the original Arabic meaning and the context of revelation before applying a verse to your life? What one specific step will you take this week to correct a past misunderstanding?

Detailed Conditions for Certainty: Lexical Meaning and Intent
Al-Taftazani details the conditions for transmitted evidence to yield knowledge, focusing on lexical meaning and intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed conditions for transmitted evidence to yield certainty, specifically the reliance on knowledge of lexical meanings through infallible transmitters of Arabic and the necessity of the speaker's intent being free from semantic ambiguities.

Core Idea
Certainty from transmission requires both correct lexical knowledge and assured intent.

Al-Taftazani delves deeper into the conditions for transmitted evidence to produce knowledge (ʿilm). He explains that knowledge of lexical meanings (waḍʿ) depends on the infallibility of transmitters of Arabic, and that knowledge of the speaker's intent (irāda) requires the absence of five specific semantic obstacles: naql, ishtirāk, majāz, iḍmār, and takhṣīṣ. This lesson unpacks these conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on knowledge of lexical meanings
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the sentence starting with 'وإنما الكلام في إفادته العلم'. Note the role of transmitters of Arabic.
The author distinguishes between uṣūl and furūʿ.
2
Understand the distinction between uṣūl and furūʿ
Notice that fundamentals (uṣūl) are clear because they are explicitly stated, while branches (furūʿ) rely on analogy (qiyās), which is probable.
3
Examine the conditions for knowing the speaker's intent
List the five conditions required for knowing intent: absence of naql, ishtirāk, majāz, iḍmār, and takhṣīṣ. Reflect on how each obstructs certainty.
Compare this list with the obstacles mentioned in the first lesson.

واعلم أن توقف النقل على ثبوت الصانع، وبعثة الأنبياء، إنما هو في الأحكام الشرعية، وفيما يقصد به حصول القطع وصحة الاحتجاج على الغير، وأما في مجرد إفادة الظن فيكفي خبر واحد أو جماعة يظن المستدل صدقه كالمنقولات عن بعض الأولياء والعلماء أو الشعراء ونحو ذلك، حتى لو جعل العلم الحاصل بالتواتر استدلالياً لم يتوقف النقل القطعي أيضاً على إثبات الصانع، وبعثة الأنبياء. قال : ولا خفاء في إفادة النقل الظن وأما إفادته اليقين فيتوقف على العام بالوضع والإرادة وذلك بعصمة رواة العربية وعدم مثل النقل والاشتراك والمجاز، والإضمار والمعارض من العقلي إذ لا بد معه من تأويل النقل لأنه فرع العقل فتكذيبه تكذيبه نعم قد ينضم إليه قرائن تنفي الاحتمال، فيفيد القطع بالمطلوب، وينفي المعارض في العقبات مثل : قل هو الله أحد، ولا إله إلا الله) . وإنما الكلام في إفادته العلم، فإنها تتوقف على العلم بوضع الألفاظ الواردة في كلام المخبر الصادق للمعاني المفهومة، وبإرادة المخبر تلك المعاني، ليلزم ثبوت المدلول والعلم بالوضع يتوقف على العلم بعصمة رواة العربية، لغة وصرفاً ونحواً عن الغلط والكذب، لأن مرجعه إلى روايتهم إذ لا طريق إلى معرفة الأوضاع سوى النقل، أما الأصول أعني ما وقع التنصيص عليه فظاهر، وأما الفروع فلأنها مبنية على الأصول بالقياس الذي هو في نفسه ظني، والعلم بالإرادة يتوقف على عدم النقل إلى معنى آخر وعلى عدم اشتراكه بين هذا المعنى وبين معنى آخر . = وعلى عدم كونه مستعملا بطريق التجوز في معنى غير الموضوع له، وعلى عدم إضمار شيء يتغير به المعنى، وعلى عدم تخصيص ما ظاهره عموم الأفراد
And know that the dependence of transmission on the establishment of the Maker and the sending of prophets is only in legal rulings and in what is intended to obtain certainty and validity of argument against others. As for merely providing probability, a single report or a group whose truthfulness the arguer thinks likely suffices, like transmitted reports from some saints, scholars, or poets and the like—even if the knowledge obtained by recurrent transmission is considered inferential, then definitive transmission does not depend on proving the Maker and sending of prophets either. Indeed, contextual indicators (qarāʾin) may accompany it that remove probability, so it yields certainty about the desired conclusion and removes the counter-evidence in rational matters, such as 'Say: He is God, One' and 'There is no god but God.' The real discussion is about its providing knowledge, for that depends on knowledge of the lexical meanings of the words that appear in the speech of the truthful informant for the understood meanings, and on the informant's intending those meanings, so that the reality of the referent is established. When Does Transmitted Evidence Depend on Reason and Prophethood?
Al-Taftazani delineates the conditional dependence of naql on reason and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which transmitted evidence (naql) depends on reason and prophethood, and the requirements for naql to produce certainty.

Core Idea
Not all transmitted evidence needs prior proof of God; only legal rulings and certainty-seeking arguments do.

Al-Taftazani begins this section by clarifying a crucial methodological point: the relationship between reason and transmitted evidence. He explains that while reason underlies all valid arguments, not every form of transmitted evidence requires prior establishment of the Creator and prophethood. This passage distinguishes between cases where naql can stand alone (for probability) and where it must be grounded in reason (for certainty).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two contexts
Look for the contrast between 'legal rulings and what aims at certainty' and 'mere production of probability'. Notice how the dependence requirement differs.
The key phrase is 'إنما هو في الأحكام الشرعية وفيما يقصد به حصول القطع'.
3
Examine the conditions for certainty
The passage lists several conditions for naql to yield certainty: knowledge of word coinage (wad'), intent (irada), infallibility ('isma) of Arabic transmitters, and absence of transposition, homonymy, metaphor, implied omission, and rational opposition. Note why rational opposition requires interpretation.
Understand that naql is 'branch of reason' (فرع العقل).
4
Reflect on the role of contextual indications
Notice that despite these strict conditions, contextual indications (qara'in) can negate probability and yield certainty, as in clear verses like 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تعاضد العقل والنقل كان المثبت ما أفاد العلم أو لا. واعلم أن توقف النقل على ثبوت الصانع، وبعثة الأنبياء، إنما هو في الأحكام الشرعية، وفيما يقصد به حصول القطع وصحة الاحتجاج على الغير، وأما في مجرد إفادة الظن فيكفي خبر واحد أو جماعة يظن المستدل صدقه كالمنقولات عن بعض الأولياء والعلماء أو الشعراء ونحو ذلك، حتى لو جعل العلم الحاصل بالتواتر استدلالياً لم يتوقف النقل القطعي أيضاً على إثبات الصانع، وبعثة الأنبياء. قال: ولا خفاء في إفادة النقل الظن، وأما إفادته اليقين فيتوقف على العلم بالوضع والإرادة وذلك بعصمة رواة العربية وعدم مثل النقل والاشتراك والمجاز، والإضمار والمعارض من العقلي إذ لا بد معه من تأويل النقل لأنه فرع العقل فتكذيبه تكذيبه نعم قد ينضم إليه قرائن تنفي الاحتمال، فيفيد القطع بالمطلوب، وينفي المعارض في العقبات مثل: قل هو الله أحد، ولا إله إلا الله.
When reason and transmitted evidence support each other, the established thing is what yields knowledge or not. Know that the dependence of transmitted evidence on the establishment of the Creator and the sending of prophets is only in legal rulings and in what is intended by it to obtain certainty and the validity of argumentation against others. As for merely producing probability, a single report or a group whose truthfulness the arguer presumes suffices, like transmitted accounts from some saints, scholars, or poets, and the like. Even if the knowledge obtained by tawatur is made argumentative, definitive transmitted evidence also does not depend on establishing the Creator and the sending of prophets. He said: There is no hiddenness in transmitted evidence producing probability. As for its producing certainty, it depends on knowledge of the linguistic coinage and intent, which requires the infallibility of the transmitters of Arabic and the absence of such things as transposition, homonymy, metaphor, implied omission, and rational opposition, for with it there must be interpretation of transmitted evidence because it is a branch of reason, so its falsification is falsification of it. Yes, there may be accompanying contextual indications that negate probability, so it yields certainty of the sought, and negates opposition in the [matters] such as 'Say: He is Allah, the One, and there is no god but Allah.'
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage clarifies the varying dependence of transmitted evidence (naql) on reason and prophethood. It begins by stating that when reason and naql mutually support each other, the resulting affirmation either yields knowledge (ilm) or not, depending on conditions. It then distinguishes: for legal rulings and for purposes requiring certainty and valid argumentation, naql depends on establishing the existence of the Creator and the sending of prophets. However, for merely producing probability (zann), a single report or group report whose truthfulness the arguer presumes suffices—even reports from saints, scholars, or poets. Even knowledge from tawatur (mass transmission) does not require that prior establishment if considered argumentative. The author then notes that naql's production of probability is clear, but its production of certainty (yaqin) depends on knowledge of word coinage (wad') and intent (irada), which in turn requires the infallibility ('isma) of Arabic transmitters from error and falsehood, and the absence of transposition (naql), homonymy (ishtirak), metaphor (majaz), implied omission (idmar), and rational opposition (mu'arid 'aqli). Since naql is a branch of reason, falsifying reason falsifies naql. However, sometimes contextual indications (qara'in) negate probability and yield certainty, as in clear verses like 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad'.

إضمار (idmar)
معارض عقلي (mu'arid 'aqli)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All transmitted evidence requires prior proof of God and prophethood.
Correction: The passage clarifies that this dependence is only for legal rulings and certainty-oriented arguments; for mere probability, naql can stand alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does transmitted evidence (naql) NOT depend on establishing the existence of the Creator and prophethood?
A) When it is used for legal rulings
B) When it is used to produce mere probability (zann)
C) When it is used in argumentation against others
D) When it is definitive (qat'i)

Key Takeaway
Transmitted evidence depends on reason and prophethood only for legal rulings and certainty-oriented arguments, not for mere probability.

Which area of your faith might be built on mere probability, and what action will you take to seek certainty?

The Two Pillars of Certainty from Transmitted Evidence
Al-Taftazani states the two main dependencies for transmitted certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two essential prerequisites for transmitted evidence to yield certainty: knowledge of word coinage and knowledge of the speaker's intent, and the first condition for knowing coinage.

Core Idea
Certainty from transmitted evidence requires knowing both the word's meaning and the speaker's intention.

Al-Taftazani begins this important section by laying out the foundational requirements for transmitted evidence (naql) to produce certain knowledge (ilm). He identifies two conditions: first, we must know the original coinage (wad') of the words used, and second, we must know that the speaker intended those meanings. He then explains that knowledge of coinage itself depends on the infallibility of Arabic transmitters.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the selected passage carefully. Notice how al-Taftazani states the two conditions in the first sentence.
2
Identify the two conditions
Look for the phrases 'العلم بوضع الألفاظ' and 'بإرادة المخبر'. These are the two pillars.
3
Understand the dependency for coinage
The passage says knowledge of coinage depends on the infallibility ('isma) of transmitters. Why is that? The commentary explains.
This is crucial because naql is the only way to know coinages.
4
Ponder the implication
If we cannot be certain of the transmitters' infallibility, then knowledge of coinage becomes uncertain. How does that affect the certainty of transmitted evidence?
This sets the stage for the rest of the passage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما الكلام في إفادته العلم، فإنها تتوقف على العلم بوضع الألفاظ الواردة في كلام المخبر الصادق للمعاني المفهومة، وبإرادة المخبر تلك المعاني، ليلزم ثبوت المدلول والعلم بالوضع يتوقف على العلم بعصمة رواة العربية، لغة وصرفاً ونحواً عن الغلط والكذب، لأن مرجعه إلى روايتهم إذ لا طريق إلى معرفة الأوضاع سوى النقل، أما الأصول أعني ما وقع التنصيص عليه فظاهر، وأما الفروع فلأنها مبنية على الأصول بالقياس الذي هو في نفسه ظني، والعلم بالإرادة يتوقف على عدم النقل إلى معنى آخر وعلى عدم اشتراكه بين هذا المعنى وبين معنى آخر. وعلى عدم كونه مستعملا بطريق التجوز في معنى غير الموضوع له، وعلى عدم إضمار شيء يتغير به المعنى، وعلى عدم تخصيص ما ظاهره عموم الأفراد أو الأوقات بالبعض من ذلك بأن يراد من أول الأمر ذلك البعض، أو يراد ما يفيد بيان انتهاء وقت الحكم، ويسمى ناسخ، وعلى عدم تقديم وتأخير بغير المعنى المطلوب عن ظاهره وفي بعض كتب الإمام وعلى عدم الحذف وفسر الحذف بأن يكون في الكلام زيادة يجب حذفها لتحصيل المعنى المقصود، كقوله تعالى: وحرام على قرية أهلكناها أنهم لا يرجعون وقوله تعالى: لا أقسم بيوم القيامة. فإن كلمة لا في الموضعين محذوفة أي واجبة الحذف، وكثير من الناس يفهمون منه أن يكون في الكلام محذوف يجب تقديره ليحصل المعنى، ويفرقون بينه وبين الإضمار بأن المضمر ما يبقى له أثر في اللفظ، كقولك خبر مقدم بإضمار قدمت. وبالجملة فلا سبيل إلى الجزم بوجود الشرائط وعدم الموانع، بل غايته الظن، وما يبتنى على الظن لا يفيد إلا الظن.
The discussion is about its producing knowledge, for it depends on knowledge of the coinage of the words occurring in the speech of the truthful informer for the understood meanings, and on the informer intending those meanings, so that the established meaning must follow. Knowledge of coinage depends on knowledge of the infallibility of the transmitters of Arabic, in lexicon, morphology, and syntax, from error and falsehood, because its reference is to their transmission since there is no way to know the coinages except through transmission. As for the principles, meaning what has been explicitly stated, it is apparent; as for the branches, they are based on principles by analogy which is in itself probable. Knowledge of intent depends on the absence of transposition to another meaning, and on the absence of homonymy between this meaning and another meaning, and on its not being used metaphorically in a meaning other than the coined one, and on the absence of an implied omission that changes the meaning, and on the absence of specification of what is apparent in generality of individuals or times to some of that, either by intending from the outset that part, or by intending what indicates the end of the ruling's time—called abrogating (nasikh)—and on the absence of forward or backward displacement contrary to the intended meaning from its apparent sense. In some books of the Imam: and on the absence of deletion (hadhf), and deletion is explained as there being an addition in the speech that must be deleted to obtain the intended meaning, like His statement, 'And forbidden is a town which We destroyed, that they will not return' and His statement, 'Nay, I swear by the Day of Resurrection.' For the word 'la' in both places is deleted, i.e., obligatory to delete. Many people understand from this that there is something omitted in the speech that must be supplied to obtain the meaning, and they differentiate between it and implied omission (idmar) in that what is implied (mudmar) leaves a trace in the wording, like your saying 'the news is fronted' by implying 'I made front'. In summary, there is no way to be certain of the existence of the conditions and the absence of obstacles; rather, its utmost is probability, and what is based on probability yields only probability.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage enumerates the conditions necessary for transmitted evidence (naql) to yield certainty (ilm). It begins by stating that for naql to produce scientific knowledge, one must know the coinage (wad') of the words in the truthful informer's speech and that the informer intended those meanings. Knowledge of coinage requires knowing the infallibility ('isma) of Arabic transmitters in lexicon, morphology, and syntax from error and falsehood, since no other means of knowing coinages exists except transmission. The author then explains: principles (usul) that are explicitly stated are clear, but branches (furu') are built on principles through analogy (qiyas), which is itself probable. Knowledge of intent (irada) depends on the absence of: transposition (naql) to another meaning, homonymy (ishtirak), metaphorical usage (tajawuz), implied omission (idmar) that changes meaning, specification (takhsis) of what is apparently general in individuals or times through initial intention or abrogation (naskh), and forward/backward displacement (taqdim wa ta'khir). Additionally, some books mention absence of deletion (hadhf), defined as a necessary addition in speech that must be deleted. The author provides Quranic examples to illustrate deletion and distinguishes it from idmar by noting that mudmar leaves a trace in the wording. The passage concludes that it is impossible to be certain of the existence of all these conditions and the absence of obstacles; the utmost is probability (zann), and what is based on probability yields only probability.

Key Terms
نقل (naql)
علم (ilm)
وضع (wad')
إرادة (irada)
عصمة ('isma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two prerequisites for transmitted evidence to yield certainty?
A) Knowledge of coinage and knowledge of the informer's intent
B) Knowledge of Arabic grammar and knowledge of logic
C) Knowledge of the transmitter's lineage and knowledge of the text's chain
D) Knowledge of the literal meaning and knowledge of the allegorical meaning

Key Takeaway
Transmitted certainty requires both knowledge of the original meanings of words and assurance that the speaker intended those meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you feel a deep certainty that you understand what Allah intended? How does the chain of transmission of the Arabic language affect that certainty, and have you ever examined the reliability of that chain?
?
If you discovered that a key term in a hadith had a different meaning at the time of the Prophet than what you assumed, would that shake your confidence? Are you willing to investigate the original coinage of words central to your faith?
?
This passage says you cannot know the meaning of revelation unless you know both the coinage and the informer's intention. Are you certain that you know what Allah intended by His words, or are you imposing your own meanings? What will you do today to ensure you are not deceiving yourself?

The Role of Analogy and the Difference Between Principles and Branches
Al-Taftazani distinguishes explicitly stated principles from derived branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why branches of transmitted evidence are based on analogy and why analogy only yields probability.

Core Idea
Explicit principles are certain, but branches rely on probabilistic analogy.

After establishing the conditions for knowing coinage, al-Taftazani distinguishes between principles (usul) that are explicitly stated in the sources and branches (furu') that are derived from principles through analogy. He notes that analogy is inherently probable, not certain, which limits the certainty of derived rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read al-Taftazani's succinct statement about usul and furu'.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice the contrast: 'أما الأصول فظاهر' and 'أما الفروع فإنها مبنية على الأصول بالقياس'.
3
Understand the nature of analogy
The author explicitly says analogy is 'ظني' (probable). Why does that matter for certainty?
If branches are built on probable reasoning, they cannot yield certainty.
4
Reflect on the implications
How does this affect the overall claim that transmitted evidence yields only probability?
This ties into the final conclusion.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all transmitted rulings are equally certain.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only explicitly stated principles are apparent/clear; branches based on analogy are only probable.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the status of analogy (qiyas)?
A) Certain
B) Probable
C) Impossible
D) Dependent on the text

Key Takeaway
Only explicitly stated principles can be known with certainty; branches derived via analogy are inherently probable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief or practice you hold that is based on a scholar's analogy rather than a clear text. Are you comfortable treating it as only probable? What would it take for you to seek out the explicit principle yourself?
?
When you hear a fatwa that says 'this is the correct view', do you assume it is certain? How does knowing that many rulings are based on probable analogy change the way you should receive such teachings?
?
If analogy yields only probability, then even the strongest scholarly consensus (ijma') on derived issues is built on probable foundations. Does that trouble you? What should your relationship be with scholarly opinions in light of this?

Conditions for Knowing Intent: Part 1 – Absence of Transposition, Homonymy, Metaphor, and Implied Omission
Al-Taftazani lists four conditions for knowing the speaker's intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four conditions that must be met to be certain the speaker intended the literal meaning: no transposition, no homonymy, no metaphorical usage, and no implied omission.

Core Idea
Knowing the speaker's intent requires ruling out common linguistic ambiguities.

After discussing knowledge of coinage, al-Taftazani turns to the second pillar: knowledge of the informer's intent. He lists a series of conditions that must all be absent for us to be certain that the speaker meant the literal, coined meaning. This lesson covers the first four: no transposition (naql), no homonymy (ishtirak), no metaphorical usage (tajawuz), and no implied omission (idmar) that changes the meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic list of conditions
Read the passage starting from 'العلم بالإرادة يتوقف على عدم النقل' up to 'إضمار شيء يتغير به المعنى'.
2
Identify each condition
There are four: عدم النقل, عدم الاشتراك, عدم التجوز, عدم الإضمار. Note what each means.
3
Understand the threat to certainty
If any of these are present, the apparent meaning may not be what is intended. Why does that undermine certainty?
4
Ponder real-life examples
Can you think of examples where a word has been transferred to another meaning, or where homonymy causes ambiguity?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Which condition deals with a word having multiple possible meanings?
A) Transposition
B) Homonymy
C) Metaphor
D) Implied omission

Key Takeaway
Certainty about the speaker's intent requires ruling out transposition, homonymy, metaphorical usage, and implied omission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran or a hadith, do you assume your immediate understanding is correct, or do you pause to consider if transposition, homonymy, metaphor, or omission might be at play? What would change if you habitually checked for these obstacles?
?
Think of a time you misunderstood someone because you took their words literally when they were being metaphorical. How does that experience challenge your confidence in interpreting sacred texts, and what will you do to avoid such mistakes going forward?
?
If you cannot be certain of a speaker's intent even in everyday conversations, how much more careful must you be with the words of Allah? Are you willing to learn the tools that scholars use to resolve these ambiguities, or do you prefer to stay comfortable with your first impression?
