Conditions for Knowing Intent: Part 2 – Specification, Abrogation, and Displacement
Al-Taftazani lists three more conditions for knowing intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional conditions for knowing the speaker's intent: no specification of a general term, no abrogation, and no forward/backward displacement.

Core Idea
General terms may be specified, rulings may be abrogated, and word order may be rearranged, all affecting intended meaning.

Al-Taftazani continues his list of conditions for knowing the informer's intent. In this lesson, we cover the next three: no specification (takhsis) that limits a general term, no abrogation (naskh) that supersedes a previous ruling, and no forward or backward displacement (taqdim wa ta'khir) that alters the apparent meaning. These are common linguistic and legal phenomena that can prevent us from taking the literal text at face value.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for these conditions
Read from 'وعلى عدم تخصيص ما ظاهره عموم' to 'بغير المعنى المطلوب عن ظاهره'.
2
Identify each condition
The three are: عدم التخصيص, عدم النسخ, عدم التقديم والتأخير. Note the explanations.
3
Understand the effect on certainty
If any of these are present, the apparent meaning does not reflect the actual intended meaning. Why?
4
Consider the Quranic examples
The passage later gives examples for deletion; but here, think of examples of specification or abrogation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما الكلام في إفادته العلم، فإنها تتوقف على العلم بوضع الألفاظ الواردة في كلام المخبر الصادق للمعاني المفهومة، وبإرادة المخبر تلك المعاني، ليلزم ثبوت المدلول والعلم بالوضع يتوقف على العلم بعصمة رواة العربية، لغة وصرفاً ونحواً عن الغلط والكذب، لأن مرجعه إلى روايتهم إذ لا طريق إلى معرفة الأوضاع سوى النقل، أما الأصول أعني ما وقع التنصيص عليه فظاهر، وأما الفروع فلأنها مبنية على الأصول بالقياس الذي هو في نفسه ظني، والعلم بالإرادة يتوقف على عدم النقل إلى معنى آخر وعلى عدم اشتراكه بين هذا المعنى وبين معنى آخر. وعلى عدم كونه مستعملا بطريق التجوز في معنى غير الموضوع له، وعلى عدم إضمار شيء يتغير به المعنى، وعلى عدم تخصيص ما ظاهره عموم الأفراد أو الأوقات بالبعض من ذلك بأن يراد من أول الأمر ذلك البعض، أو يراد ما يفيد بيان انتهاء وقت الحكم، ويسمى ناسخ، وعلى عدم تقديم وتأخير بغير المعنى المطلوب عن ظاهره وفي بعض كتب الإمام وعلى عدم الحذف وفسر الحذف بأن يكون في الكلام زيادة يجب حذفها لتحصيل المعنى المقصود، كقوله تعالى: وحرام على قرية أهلكناها أنهم لا يرجعون وقوله تعالى: لا أقسم بيوم القيامة. فإن كلمة لا في الموضعين محذوفة أي واجبة الحذف، وكثير من الناس يفهمون منه أن يكون في الكلام محذوف يجب تقديره ليحصل المعنى، ويفرقون بينه وبين الإضمار بأن المضمر ما يبقى له أثر في اللفظ، كقولك خبر مقدم بإضمار قدمت. وبالجملة فلا سبيل إلى الجزم بوجود الشرائط وعدم الموانع، بل غايته الظن، وما يبتنى على الظن لا يفيد إلا الظن.
The discussion is about its producing knowledge, for it depends on knowledge of the coinage of the words occurring in the speech of the truthful informer for the understood meanings, and on the informer intending those meanings, so that the established meaning must follow. Knowledge of coinage depends on knowledge of the infallibility of the transmitters of Arabic, in lexicon, morphology, and syntax, from error and falsehood, because its reference is to their transmission since there is no way to know the coinages except through transmission. As for the principles, meaning what has been explicitly stated, it is apparent; as for the branches, they are based on principles by analogy which is in itself probable. Knowledge of intent depends on the absence of transposition to another meaning, and on the absence of homonymy between this meaning and another meaning, and on its not being used metaphorically in a meaning other than the coined one, and on the absence of an implied omission that changes the meaning, and on the absence of specification of what is apparent in generality of individuals or times to some of that, either by intending from the outset that part, or by intending what indicates the end of the ruling's time—called abrogating (nasikh)—and on the absence of forward or backward displacement contrary to the intended meaning from its apparent sense. In some books of the Imam: and on the absence of deletion (hadhf), and deletion is explained as there being an addition in the speech that must be deleted to obtain the intended meaning, like His statement, 'And forbidden is a town which We destroyed, that they will not return' and His statement, 'Nay, I swear by the Day of Resurrection.' For the word 'la' in both places is deleted, i.e., obligatory to delete. Many people understand from this that there is something omitted in the speech that must be supplied to obtain the meaning, and they differentiate between it and implied omission (idmar) in that what is implied (mudmar) leaves a trace in the wording, like your saying 'the news is fronted' by implying 'I made front'. In summary, there is no way to be certain of the existence of the conditions and the absence of obstacles; rather, its utmost is probability, and what is based on probability yields only probability.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage enumerates the conditions necessary for transmitted evidence (naql) to yield certainty (ilm). It begins by stating that for naql to produce scientific knowledge, one must know the coinage (wad') of the words in the truthful informer's speech and that the informer intended those meanings. Knowledge of coinage requires knowing the infallibility ('isma) of Arabic transmitters in lexicon, morphology, and syntax from error and falsehood, since no other means of knowing coinages exists except transmission. The author then explains: principles (usul) that are explicitly stated are clear, but branches (furu') are built on principles through analogy (qiyas), which is itself probable. Knowledge of intent (irada) depends on the absence of: transposition (naql) to another meaning, homonymy (ishtirak), metaphorical usage (tajawuz), implied omission (idmar) that changes meaning, specification (takhsis) of what is apparently general in individuals or times through initial intention or abrogation (naskh), and forward/backward displacement (taqdim wa ta'khir). Additionally, some books mention absence of deletion (hadhf), defined as a necessary addition in speech that must be deleted. The author provides Quranic examples to illustrate deletion and distinguishes it from idmar by noting that mudmar leaves a trace in the wording. The passage concludes that it is impossible to be certain of the existence of all these conditions and the absence of obstacles; the utmost is probability (zann), and what is based on probability yields only probability.

Key Terms
تخصيص (takhsis)
نسخ (naskh)
تقديم وتأخير (taqdim wa ta'khir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'naskh' refer to in this passage?
A) Transposition of meaning
B) Abrogation of a ruling
C) Metaphorical usage
D) Implied omission

Key Takeaway
Specification, abrogation, and displacement are further obstacles to taking apparent meanings as intended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that commands something seemingly universal, do you stop to consider whether it might be specified or abrogated? If not, what does that say about your commitment to understanding Allah's actual intent?
?
Have you ever misinterpreted someone's statement because you assumed the words were in their natural order, only to realize later that the speaker had rearranged them for emphasis? How might this lesson prevent you from misinterpreting divine speech?
?
Knowing that scholars have rigorously identified conditions like specification and abrogation to understand the Quran, what effort have you personally made to learn these tools instead of relying on simplistic readings?

The Condition of Deletion (Hadhf) and the Final Verdict on Certainty
Al-Taftazani discusses deletion and concludes transmitted evidence yields only probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition of deletion (hadhf), its distinction from implied omission (idmar), and why transmitted evidence ultimately cannot yield certainty.

Core Idea
Even deletion can obscure intended meaning, and overall certainty is unattainable.

In this final lesson on the conditions for transmitted evidence, al-Taftazani discusses an additional condition mentioned in some books: the absence of deletion (hadhf). He explains its definition with Quranic examples and distinguishes it from idmar. He then concludes the entire section with the sobering statement that it is impossible to be certain of all these conditions, so transmitted evidence only yields probability.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion on deletion
Read from 'وفي بعض كتب الإمام وعلى عدم الحذف' to the end of the passage.
2
Understand hadhf vs. idmar
Follow the distinction: hadhf is an addition that must be deleted, idmar is an omission that leaves a trace. Note the examples.
3
Read the concluding sentence
The final sentence: 'وبالجملة فلا سبيل إلى الجزم...'
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Why does the author conclude that transmitted evidence cannot yield certainty? List the many conditions.

?

?

?

Transmitted Evidence Can Yield Certainty
States that transmitted evidence can be certain through tawatur and clear contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how transmitted evidence can produce certainty when supported by tawatur and clear contextual clues.

Core Idea
Transmitted evidence yields certainty when it is mutawatir and contextually clear.

After refuting the view that transmitted evidence is always uncertain, the author presents the correct position: such evidence can be certain. He demonstrates this by showing how tawatur and contextual clues remove doubt, citing examples from language and Quranic texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the author's confident tone as he asserts the truth.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence that states the correct view. It begins with 'والحق أن الدليل النقلي قد يفيد القطع'. Understand that this is the thesis.
3
Examine the two ways certainty is achieved
Look for how tawatur (recurrent transmission) establishes certainty for words and grammar, and how contextual clues (qara'in) provide certainty for intended meanings.
The author gives specific examples for each.
4
Reflect on the Quranic examples
The Quranic verses are cited as examples where mere hearing (sam') suffices for certainty. Consider why these verses are considered clear.
Notice the verses cover tawhid and resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا والحق أن الدليل النقلي قد يفيد القطع إذ من الأوضاع معلوم بطريق التواتر كلفظ السماء والأرض، وكأكثر قواعد الصرف والنحو في وضع هيئات المفردات وهيئات التراكيب، والعلم بالإرادة يحصل بمعونة القرائن بحيث لا تبقى شبهة كما في النصوص الواردة في إيجاب الصلاة والزكاة ونحوهما وفي التوحيد والبعث وإذا اكتفينا فيهما بمجرد السمع كقوله تعالى قل هو الله أحد فاعلم أنه لا إله إلا الله قل يحييها الذي أنشأها أول مرة وهو بكل خلق عليم
This [is the correct view]. And the truth is that transmitted evidence may yield certainty, for among the conventions [that are] known by way of recurrent transmission (tawātur) is the word 'sky' and 'earth', and most of the rules of morphology and syntax regarding the forms of individual words and the forms of constructions. And knowledge of the intended meaning is obtained with the help of contextual clues (qarāʾin) such that no doubt remains, as in the texts regarding the obligation of prayer, zakāh, and the like, and regarding tawḥīd and resurrection. And if we suffice in these matters with mere hearing (samʿ) – like His saying, Exalted is He: 'Say: He is Allah, [the] One' (Q. 112:1), 'Know that there is no god but Allah' (Q. 47:19), 'Say: He will give them life who created them the first time, and He is, of every creation, Knowing' (Q. 36:79).
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the correct position (al-ḥaqq) after the refutation. The author asserts that transmitted evidence (al-dalīl al-naqlī) can indeed yield certainty (qaṭʿ). He supports this with several points: (1) Certain words like 'sky' and 'earth' are known through tawātur (mass, uninterrupted transmission) in terms of their meanings. (2) Most rules of Arabic morphology and syntax are firmly established through widespread usage. (3) The intended meaning of a statement can be known with the help of contextual clues (qarāʾin) that remove all doubt, as in the clear texts (nuṣūṣ) that obligate prayer and zakāh, and those that affirm tawḥīd (divine unity) and resurrection (baʿth). (4) In such cases, even relying solely on hearing (samʿ) – i.e., the transmitted report – is sufficient for certainty, as exemplified by the three Quranic verses cited. So the author demonstrates that transmitted evidence is not inherently conjectural; when conditions like tawātur and clear context are met, it can produce certain knowledge.

Key Terms
تواتر
قرائن
قطع
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transmitted evidence never yields certainty.
Correction: The passage shows that when conditions like tawatur and clear context are met, transmitted evidence can indeed produce certainty.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is one example of transmitted evidence that yields certainty?
A) The words 'sky' and 'earth' as known through tawātur.
B) Any isolated hadith.
C) Metaphorical Quranic verses.
D) All cases of transmitted evidence.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted evidence can yield certainty when it is mutawatir or supported by clear contextual clues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Say: He is Allah, One,' do you actually feel certain that this is the truth, or do you allow doubts to creep in? What would it take for you to say, like the author, that this text with its context leaves no room for doubt? Commit to reading it tomorrow with full conviction.
?
Think of a clear command in the Quran or Sunnah (like prayer or zakah) that you know is mutawatir. Have you been treating it as optional or negotiable? The author says such texts yield certainty—does your practice reflect that certainty? If not, what change will you make today?
?
The author argues that transmitted evidence can be certain. Is there an area of your faith where you rely solely on transmitted knowledge but feel uncertainty? Why? What contextual clue or tawatur are you missing? Examine your heart and identify one step to remove that doubt.

Answering the Objection of Potential Rational Contradiction
Answers the objection that rational contradiction always remains possible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars answer the objection that rational contradiction always remains possible, distinguishing between legal and theological matters.

Core Idea
The objection fails: in legal matters reason has no independent role, in theology we know the three conditions.

This passage addresses a key skeptical objection: how can we be sure that no rational argument contradicts a revealed text? The author systematically answers by separating legal rulings, where reason does not independently legislate, from theological truths, where we can verify the knowledge of God's assignment, intention, and truthfulness. By understanding this distinction, the student sees the coherence of relying on transmitted evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: 'The possibility of a contradictory proof remains...' Identify the skeptic's point.
They claim even with contextual clues we can't be certain.
2
Follow the answer for legal matters
Note the author's response: reason has no scope in these matters, so no rational contradiction can arise. The negation of a contradictory from revelation is known necessarily from religion.
Examples: prayer and zakah.
3
Follow the answer for rational matters
Here knowledge of the absence of contradiction comes from knowing three things: the linguistic assignment, the intended meaning, and the truthfulness of the informer.
These are all present in clear texts of tawhid.
4
Reflect on the overall answer
See how the objection is defeated by showing either reason has no role or we have sufficient knowledge.
This preserves the authority of revealed texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : احتمال المعارض قائم، إذ لا جزم بعدمه بمجرد الدليل النقلي، أو بمعونة القرائن . ما هو قلنا : أما في الشرعيات فلا خفاء، إذ لا مجال للعقل فلا معارض من قبله، ونفي المعارض من قبل الشرع معلوم بالضرورة من الدين، في مثل ما ذكرنا من الصلاة والزكاة . وأما في العقليات فلأن العلم بنفي المعارض العقلي، حاصل عند العلم بالوضع والإرادة وصدق المخبر على ما هو المفروض في نصوص التوحيد.
For if it is said: The possibility of a contradictory [proof] remains, for there is no certainty of its non-existence merely by the transmitted evidence, or with the help of contextual clues – what is [the answer]? We say: As for legal matters (sharʿiyyāt), there is no obscurity, for reason has no [independent] scope, so there is no contradictory from its side, and the negation of a contradictory from the side of revelation is known by necessity from religion, in the likes of what we mentioned of prayer and zakāh. And as for rational matters (ʿaqliyyāt), [it is] because knowledge of the absence of a rational contradictory is obtained when there is knowledge of the linguistic assignment (waḍʿ), the intended meaning (irāda), and the truthfulness of the informer (ṣidq al-mukhbiʾ) – as is the case in the texts of tawḥīd.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection (al-qīl) contends that the possibility of a rational contradiction (iḥtimāl al-muʿāriḍ) always remains; we cannot be certain of its non-existence just from the transmitted evidence, even with contextual clues. The answer (qulnā) has two parts: (1) In sharʿiyyāt (legal matters such as prayer and zakāh), there is no difficulty because reason has no independent role in these areas, and the absence of a contradictory from revelation is known as a necessary (ḍarūrī) matter from religion itself. (2) In ʿaqliyyāt (theological matters like tawḥīd), we have knowledge that no rational contradiction exists when we know three things: the linguistic assignment (waḍʿ), the intended meaning (irāda), and the truthfulness of the informer (ṣidq al-mukhbiʾ) – and these are all given in the clear texts of tawḥīd. Thus, the objection is answered.

Key Terms
شرعيات
عقليات
ضرورة الدين
صدق المخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies any rational role in legal matters.
Correction: It only denies reason's independent scope for contradiction; reason still plays a role in understanding and applying legal texts.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is there no possibility of contradiction in sharʿiyyāt (legal matters)?
A) Because reason always agrees with revelation.
B) Because reason has no scope for independent judgment in these matters.
C) Because transmitted evidence in legal matters is always certain.
D) Because contextual clues remove all doubt.

Key Takeaway
The objection of potential rational contradiction is answered by distinguishing legal matters (where reason has no independent role) from theological matters (where we have knowledge of waḍʿ, irāda, and ṣidq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt about a religious ruling seeming unreasonable, do you remember that in legal matters reason has no independent role? How does that change your approach to such doubts?
?
The passage says we can know the absence of contradiction when we know the linguistic assignment, intended meaning, and truthfulness of the informer. Have you ever sought to understand the Quran with this awareness? What would change if you did?
?
If you ever feel shaken by an intellectual objection to tawḥīd, will you recall that the objection is answered by these three conditions? What would that look like in practice?

Transmitted Evidence Can Yield Certainty
The author argues that transmitted evidence can yield certainty and answers objections about counter-evidence and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how transmitted evidence can yield certainty and how the author refutes objections regarding counter-evidence and circularity.

Core Idea
Transmitted evidence can yield certainty when linguistic and contextual factors are clear.

In this passage, the author defends the reliability of transmitted evidence (dalīl naqlī) against skeptics who claim it can never produce certainty. He argues that with clear linguistic conventions, mass transmission, and contextual indicators, transmitted evidence can yield certainty even in rational matters. He then addresses two key objections: the possibility of counter-evidence and the charge of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage begins mid-response, addressing a prior objection. Read the opening lines to grasp the ongoing debate about reason and transmitted evidence.
The author previously discussed reason-based evidence.
2
Identify the main claim and examples
The author asserts that transmitted evidence can yield certainty, giving examples like words known by tawātur and clear scriptural texts. Note the role of contextual indicators (qarā'in).
Examples include 'heaven' and 'earth' and verses on prayer and tawḥīd.
3
Analyze the first objection and answer
The objection is that counter-evidence is always possible. The answer distinguishes legal matters (no rational counter-evidence) from rational matters (knowledge of negation via mutual exclusivity).
In legal matters, reason has no role; in rational, linguistic and contextual clarity removes doubt.
4
Analyze the second objection and answer
The objection is circularity: certainty depends on knowing counter-evidence is absent. The answer: dependence is on actual absence, not knowledge; often certainty occurs without considering counter-evidence.
The condition is that if the mind considers the counter-evidence, it would be certain of its non-existence.
5
Reflect on the final clarification
The author explains that the condition for certainty is not knowledge of absence of counter-evidence, but the actual absence and the ability to dismiss it if considered.
The passage ends with a call to ponder and reliance on Allah.

إذ لا معنى لعدم تصديقه سوى هذا ولا حاجة إلى باقي المقدمات مع ما في الحصر من المناقشة إذ لا يلزم تصديقهما أو تكذيبهما، أو تصديق أحدهما وتكذيب الآخر، لجواز أن يحكم بتساقطهما، وكونهما في حكم العدم، من غير يعتقد معهما حقية شيء أو بطلانه، ولو جعل التكذيب مساوياً لعدم التصديق لم يلزم من تكذيب العقل والنقل اعتقاد ارتفاع النقيضين وبطلانهما، لأن معنى عدم تصديق الدليل عدم اعتقاد صحته واستلزامه لحقية النتيجة، وهذا لا يستلزم بطلانها أو اعتقاد بطلانها وارتفاعها فغاية الأمر التوقف في الإثبات والنفي، على أن تكذيبهما أيضاً يستلزم المطلوب، أعني عدم إفادة النقل العلم فنفيه يكون مستدركاً في البيان هذا والحق أن الدليل النقلي قد يفيد القطع إذ من الأوضاع معلوم بطريق التواتر كلفظ السماء والأرض، وكأكثر قواعد الصرف والنحو في وضع هيئات المفردات وهيئات التراكيب، والعلم بالإرادة يحصل بمعونة القرائن بحيث لا تبقى شبهة كما في النصوص الواردة في إيجاب الصلاة والزكاة ونحوهما وفي التوحيد والبعث وإذا اكتفينا فيهما بمجرد السمع كقوله تعالى قل هو الله أحد فاعلم أنه لا إله إلا الله قل يحييها الذي أنشأها أول مرة وهو بكل خلق عليم. فإن قيل : احتمال المعارض قائم، إذ لا جزم بعدمه بمجرد الدليل النقلي، أو بمعونة القرائن . قلنا : أما في الشرعيات فلا خفاء، إذ لا مجال للعقل فلا معارض من قبله، ونفي المعارض من قبل الشرع معلوم بالضرورة من الدين، في مثل ما ذكرنا من الصلاة والزكاة . وأما في العقليات فلأن العلم بنفي المعارض العقلي، حاصل عند العلم بالوضع والإرادة وصدق المخبر على ما هو المفروض في نصوص التوحيد، والبعث وذلك لأن العلم بتحقق أحد المتنافيين يفيد العلم بانتفاء المنافي الآخر، كما سبق في إفادة النظر العلم بالمطلوب وبانتفاء المعارض . فإن قيل : إفادتها اليقين على العلم تتوقف على العلم بنفي المعارض، فإثباته بها يكون دوراً . قلنا : إنما يثبت بها التصديق بحصول هذا العلم بناء على حصول ملزومه، على أن الحق إن إفاد العلم إنما يتوقف على انتفاء المعارض وعدم اعتقاد ثبوته، لا على العلم بانتفائه إذ كثيراً ما يحصل اليقين من الدليل ولا يخطر المعارض بالبال إثباتاً أو نفياً، فضلاً عن العلم بذلك. فما يقال : إن إفادة اليقين تكون مع العلم بنفي المعارض وأنه يفيد ذلك ويستلزمه فمعناه أنه يكون بحيث إذا لاحظ العقل هذا المعارض جزم بانتفائه. ويدل على ما ذكرنا قطعاً ما ذكروا في بيان هذا الاشتراط، من أنه لا جزم مع المعارض بل الحاصل معه التوقف فليتأمل والله الهادي.
For there is no meaning to not believing it except this, and there is no need for the rest of the premises, along with what is in the enumeration of criticism, for it is not necessary to believe both or disbelieve both, or to believe one and disbelieve the other, because it is possible to judge that they cancel each other out and that they are in the status of non-existence, without believing with them the truth of something or its falsehood. And if disbelief were made equivalent to non-belief, it would not follow from disbelieving reason and transmitted evidence the belief in the removal of the two contradictories and their falsehood, because the meaning of not believing the evidence is not believing its correctness and its entailment of the truth of the conclusion, and this does not entail its falsehood or the belief in its falsehood and removal; rather the utmost is suspension in affirmation and negation. Moreover, disbelieving both also entails the desired [conclusion], namely that transmitted evidence does not produce knowledge, so its negation would be redundant in the statement. This is the truth: transmitted evidence may yield certainty (qat'), for among linguistic conventions is that which is known by way of tawātur (mass transmission), like the words 'heaven' and 'earth', and most rules of morphology and syntax in the assignment of forms of individual words and forms of constructions. And knowledge of the intended meaning is obtained with the aid of contextual indicators (qarā'in) such that no doubt remains, as in the texts regarding the obligation of prayer and alms and the like, and regarding tawḥīd (divine unity) and resurrection. And if we suffice in them with mere hearing (sam'), like His saying, exalted: 'Say: He is Allah, the One' (Q 112:1), 'Know that there is no god but Allah' (Q 47:19), 'Say: He who created them the first time will give them life; and He is, of all creation, Knowing' (Q 36:79). If it is said: The possibility of a counter-evidence (mu'āriḍ) exists, for there is no certainty of its non-existence merely by transmitted evidence, or with the aid of contextual indicators. We say: As for legal matters (shar'iyyāt), there is no obscurity, for there is no room for reason, so there is no counter-evidence from it; and the negation of counter-evidence from the side of the Law is known by necessity from religion, in cases like what we mentioned of prayer and alms. As for rational matters ('aqliyyāt), because knowledge of the negation of rational counter-evidence is obtained when one knows the linguistic convention (waḍ'), the intended meaning (irāda), and the truthfulness of the informant, as is presupposed in the texts on tawḥīd and resurrection. That is because knowledge of the actuality of one of two mutually exclusive things yields knowledge of the negation of the other. If it is said: Their yielding certainty ('ilm) depends on knowledge of the negation of the counter-evidence, so establishing it by them would be circular (dawr). We say: What is established by them is only the affirmation of the occurrence of this knowledge based on the occurrence of its necessary condition (malzūm). Moreover, the truth is that their yielding knowledge depends only on the absence (intifā') of the counter-evidence and not believing its existence, not on knowledge of its absence, for often certainty is obtained from evidence without the counter-evidence occurring to the mind either affirmatively or negatively, let alone knowledge of that. What is said—that the yielding of certainty is accompanied by knowledge of the negation of the counter-evidence and that it yields that and entails it—its meaning is that it is such that if the mind observes this counter-evidence, it would be certain of its non-existence. And what supports what we have said definitively is what they mentioned in explaining this condition: that there is no certainty with the counter-evidence; rather what obtains with it is suspense. So let one ponder, and Allah is the Guide.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument defending the claim that transmitted evidence (dalīl naqlī) can yield certainty (qaṭ'), particularly in rational matters, and addressing two objections: the possibility of counter-evidence (mu'āriḍ) and the charge of circularity (dawr). The passage begins in the middle of a response, arguing that disbelieving both reason and transmitted evidence does not entail belief in the removal of contradictories—since one may simply suspend judgment. The author then asserts the truth: transmitted evidence can yield certainty. He gives examples: words known by tawātur (mass transmission) like 'heaven' and 'earth', and most rules of morphology and syntax; also texts whose intended meaning is clear with contextual indicators (qarā'in), such as verses on prayer, alms, tawḥīd, and resurrection. Then an objection is raised: the possibility of a counter-evidence exists, so one cannot be certain. The answer distinguishes legal matters (shar'iyyāt), where reason has no role and the negation of counter-evidence is known by necessity, from rational matters ('aqliyyāt), where knowledge of the negation of a rational counter-evidence is obtained when one knows the linguistic convention, intended meaning, and truthfulness of the informant—since knowledge of one of two mutually exclusive things yields knowledge of the negation of the other. A second objection charges circularity: if certainty depends on knowledge of the absence of counter-evidence, but you claim the evidence itself gives that knowledge, that is circular. The author replies that what the evidence establishes is only the affirmation of this knowledge based on the occurrence of its necessary condition; and moreover, certainty depends only on the actual absence of counter-evidence, not on knowledge of that absence. He illustrates: often certainty arises without the counter-evidence occurring to the mind at all. Finally, he explains that the condition of 'certainty with knowledge of the absence of counter-evidence' means that if the mind were to consider the counter-evidence, it would be certain of its non-existence. He supports this by noting that scholars state that there is no certainty when a counter-evidence is present; only suspense. The passage closes with a call to ponder and reliance on Allah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transmitted evidence (dalīl naqlī) can never produce certainty (yaqīn) because of the possibility of counter-evidence or circularity.
Correction: The author shows that transmitted evidence can yield certainty when the linguistic convention and contextual indicators are clear, and that certainty depends on actual absence of counter-evidence, not knowledge of it.

Checkpoint

B) The actual absence (intifā') of a counter-evidence and not believing its existence
C) Complete suspension of judgment until all possible counter-evidence is examined
D) The impossibility of any counter-evidence existing

Key Takeaway
Transmitted evidence can yield certainty when linguistic and contextual factors are clear, and objections regarding counter-evidence and circularity are answerable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected or doubted a clear religious command because a weak counterargument popped into your mind? What would it look like to treat that counterargument as irrelevant unless it is actually present and strong?
?
The author says certainty often arises without the counter-evidence even crossing your mind. Think of a belief you hold with full certainty—perhaps in Allah's existence or the Qur'an's truth. Is that certainty built on deliberate scrutiny of every possible doubt, or on the overwhelming clarity of the evidence itself? What would change if you trusted that clarity more?
?

Is the evidence truly unclear, or is the lack of certainty a reflection of how deeply you have engaged with it? Are you willing to re-examine those texts until the clarity forces certainty upon you?

Refuting Circularity in the Condition of Certainty from Proof
Objection and answer about circularity in requiring knowledge of no counter-evidence for certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of circularity against the condition of certainty from proof is refuted by distinguishing between actual absence of counter-evidence and knowledge of that absence.

Core Idea
Certainty requires absence of counter-evidence, not knowledge of that absence.

This passage addresses a critical objection to the condition that certainty from a proof requires knowledge that no counter-evidence exists. The objection claims this leads to circularity. The author refutes it by clarifying that certainty depends only on the actual absence of counter-evidence and not on explicit awareness of its absence. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how proof yields certainty in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the translation. Notice the objection starts with 'If it is said' and the answer with 'We say'.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that if certainty from a proof depends on knowledge of no counter-evidence, then proving that absence by the same proof would be circular. Find this in the text.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer argues that certainty depends only on the actual absence of counter-evidence and not on knowledge of that absence. It gives the example of often obtaining certainty without the counter-evidence even occurring to the mind.
4
Understand the clarification
The author clarifies that 'certainty occurs with knowledge of the negation of the opposite' means that if the intellect were to consider the counter-evidence, it would judge it negated—not that one must have prior knowledge.
5
Reflect on the condition
Ponder the final statement: there is no certain judgment when counter-evidence is present; only suspension occurs. This supports the author's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبعث وذلك لأن العلم بتحقق أحد المتنافيين يفيد العلم بانتفاء المنافي الآخر، كما سبق في إفادة النظر العلم بالمطلوب وبانتفاء المعارض . فإن قيل : إفادتها اليقين على العلم تتوقف على العلم بنفي المعارض، فإثباته بها يكون دوراً . قلنا : إنما يثبت بها التصديق بحصول هذا العلم بناء على حصول ملزومه، على أن الحق إن إفاد العلم إنما يتوقف على انتفاء المعارض وعدم اعتقاد ثبوته، لا على العلم بانتفائه إذ كثيراً ما يحصل اليقين من الدليل ولا يخطر المعارض بالبال إثباتاً أو نفياً، فضلاً عن العلم بذلك. فما يقال : إن إفادة اليقين تكون مع العلم بنفي المعارض وأنه يفيد ذلك ويستلزمه فمعناه أنه يكون بحيث إذا لاحظ العقل هذا المعارض جزم بانتفائه. ويدل على ما ذكرنا قطعاً ما ذكروا في بيان هذا الاشتراط، من أنه لا جزم مع المعارض بل الحاصل معه التوقف فليتأمل والله الهادي .
And because knowledge of the actuality of one of two mutually exclusive things yields knowledge of the negation of the other, as previously in the benefit of theoretical reasoning (al-nazar) knowledge of the sought and of the negation of the opposite. If it is said: Its benefit of certainty depends on knowledge of the negation of the opposite, so proving it by it would be circular. We say: It only establishes the affirmation of obtaining this knowledge based on the obtaining of its necessary condition, except that the truth is that the benefit of knowledge depends only on the absence of the opposite and the non-belief in its existence, not on knowledge of its absence, for often certainty is obtained from the proof (dalil) and the opposite does not occur to the mind positively or negatively, let alone knowledge of that. So what is said: that the benefit of certainty occurs with knowledge of the negation of the opposite, and that it benefits that and necessitates it, its meaning is that it is such that when the intellect considers this opposite, it judges its negation. And what they mentioned in the explanation of this condition indicates what we have said with certainty, namely that there is no judgement with the opposite, rather what occurs with it is suspension. So ponder, and God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding a condition for certainty from a proof (dalil). The objection argues that if certainty from a proof requires knowledge that there is no counter-evidence (mu'arid), then proving that absence of counter-evidence by the same proof would be circular (dawr). The answer refutes this by clarifying that certainty depends only on the actual absence of counter-evidence and the non-belief in its existence, not on explicit knowledge of that absence. Often certainty arises without the counter-evidence even occurring to the mind. The phrase 'certainty occurs with knowledge of the negation of the opposite' means that the proof is such that if the intellect were to consider the counter-evidence, it would judge it negated—not that one must be actively aware of its negation. The passage concludes by noting that what others have said about this condition supports this view: there is no judgement (jazzm) with the presence of counter-evidence; rather, what occurs is suspension (tawaqquf).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That certainty from a proof requires conscious knowledge that no counter-evidence exists.
Correction: Certainty only requires the actual absence of counter-evidence and not actively thinking about it. The proof itself, if sound, guarantees that if counter-evidence were considered, it would be negated.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the charge of circularity in requiring knowledge of no counter-evidence for certainty?
A) By arguing that the condition is actually knowledge of the absence, not the absence itself.
B) By asserting that certainty depends only on the absence of counter-evidence, not on knowledge of that absence.
C) By stating that circularity is impossible in logical reasoning.
D) By denying that counter-evidence is relevant to certainty.

Key Takeaway
Certainty from a proof requires only the actual absence of counter-evidence, not explicit knowledge of its absence; this avoids circularity and matches common experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
In your pursuit of certainty, have you fallen into the trap of requiring yourself to have answered every possible doubt before you are certain? How will you change after learning that actual absence suffices?

Definition and Scope of al-Umur al-‘Amma
Definition and justification of the chapter on general matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what 'General Matters' (al-Umur al-‘Amma) means in kalam, why this chapter is placed before the subsequent sections, and which topics it includes and excludes.

Core Idea
General Matters study the states common to all existents: the Necessary, substance, and accident.

This passage introduces the second major section of the work, explaining what 'General Matters' are and why they deserve a separate chapter placed before the rest. The author lays out the rationale for its placement and clarifies which universal concepts belong here as opposed to logic or other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the dense style and how each sentence builds on the previous one.
Pay attention to terms like al-umur al-‘amma, al-wajib, al-jawhar, al-‘arad.
2
Identify the three categories and common states
Find the three categories of existents mentioned (wajib, jawhar, ‘arad) and the states common to all or some of them (existence, non-existence, unity, temporal origination, multiplicity).
These common states are the core subject matter of this chapter.
3
Understand the reasoning for placement and limitation
Focus on why the author places this chapter before the other four: because later sections depend on these general principles. Also note his clarification that 'most existents' refers to the three categories, not individual entities.
This step addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الأمور العامة: المقصد الثاني: في الأمور العامة، وهو ما يعم أكثر الموجودات: الواجب، والجوهر، والعرض. فيكون البحث عن العدم والامتناع بالعرض وعن الوجوب لكونه من أقسام مطلق الوجوب وبيانها في فصول. قد سبقت الإشارة إلى أن وجه تقديم هذا المقصد على الأربعة الباقية قد توقف بعض بياناتها عليه، ووجه إفراده عنها مع كونه عائداً إليها، هو أنه إنما كان البحث عن أحوال الموجود وقد انقسم إلى الواجب، والجوهر، والعرض، واختص كل منها بأحوال تعرف في بابه، احتيج إلى باب معرفة الأحوال المشتركة بين الثلاثة كالوجود والعدم والوحدة، أو الاثنين فقط، كالحدوث والكثرة. وبهذا يظهر أن المراد بالموجودات في قولهم الأمور العامة ما يعم أكثر الموجودات هو أقسامه الثلاثة التي هي الواجب، والجوهر، والعرض، لا أفراده التي لا سبيل للعقل إلى حصرها وتعيين الأكثر منها والحكم بأن مثل القلة والكثرة يعم أكثرها. ولا خفاء في أن المقصود بالنظر ما يتعلق به عرض علمي ويترتب عليه مقصود أصلي من الفن، ولا يكون له ذكر في أحد المقاصد بالأصالة، وإلا فكثير من الأمور الشاملة مما لا يبحث عنه في الباب كالكمية، والكيفية، والإضافة، والمعلومية، والمقدورية، وسائر مباحث الكليات.
On General Matters: The Second Purpose: on General Matters, which is that which encompasses most existents: the Necessary, the Substance, and the Accident. So the investigation is concerning non-existence, impossibility by accident, and concerning necessity because it is among the divisions of absolute necessity, and their explanation in chapters. The indication has already preceded that the reason for presenting this purpose before the remaining four is that some of their data depend upon it. And the reason for separating it from them despite its being related to them is that since the investigation was concerning the states of the existent and it divided into the Necessary, the Substance, and the Accident, and each of them is distinguished by states known in its proper chapter, there arose a need for a chapter for knowing the states common among the three, such as existence, non-existence, and unity, or common to only two, such as temporal origination and multiplicity. And by this it becomes apparent that what is meant by 'existents' in their statement 'general matters which encompass most existents' are its three divisions which are the Necessary, the Substance, and the Accident, not its individuals which the intellect has no way to enumerate and to determine the most numerous of them and to judge that such as fewness and manyness applies to most of them. And it is not hidden that the intention of investigation is that which pertains to a scientific property and from which arises an original purpose of the discipline, and it is not mentioned in any of the purposes originally. Otherwise, many universal things are not investigated in this chapter, such as quantity, quality, relation, knowability, powerability, and the rest of the topics of the universals.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage defines and introduces the second main section of the work, titled 'al-Umur al-‘Amma' (General Matters). The author begins by stating that this section deals with what is common to most existents, specifically the three categories: the Necessary Being (God), substance (jawhar), and accident (‘arad). He then explains that the investigation of this section includes topics like non-existence, impossibility by accident, and necessity, treated within various chapters. Next, the author explains the rationale for placing this section before the subsequent four sections: because some points in those later sections depend on the general principles established here. He then explains why this section is treated separately even though its topics are common to the later sections. The reason is that since the investigation of the existent divides into the three categories, each with its own specific states studied in its own chapter, a separate chapter is needed to study the states common among them, such as existence, non-existence, and unity (common to all three), or temporal origination and multiplicity (common only to two). Then the author clarifies that by 'most existents' they mean the three categories, not individual instances, because the intellect cannot enumerate all individuals or determine which are the majority. Finally, he emphasizes that the proper object of investigation is only those common states that yield a scientific property and serve an original purpose of the discipline; otherwise, many universal concepts like quantity, quality, etc., which are studied in logic, are not included here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'most existents' refers to a majority of individual things in the universe.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'most existents' means the three broad categories—Necessary, substance, accident—not individual entities.

Checkpoint
Why is the chapter on al-Umur al-‘Amma placed before the other four purposes?
A) Because it deals with the most important topics.
B) Because some data in the later purposes depend on the general matters explained here.
C) Because it is the longest chapter.
D) Because the author intended it as an introduction to logic.

Key Takeaway
The chapter on General Matters is foundational because it covers the common properties of the three main categories of existents: the Necessary, substance, and accident.

Reflect on a time you assumed a concept was part of a field when it actually belonged elsewhere. How does the author's careful delineation of what belongs to General Matters versus logic challenge you to be more precise in your own theological thinking?
?
The author emphasizes that only states with a scientific purpose are investigated. In your own pursuit of knowledge, are you drawn to topics that serve a real purpose, or do you sometimes get lost in details without asking why they matter? What will you change after reading this lesson?

Definition of Universal Matters
Sanusi defines universal matters and states their scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of universal matters and what they encompass according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
Universal matters are the shared states of the three categories of existent: necessary, substance, and accident.

Sanusi begins the second purpose by defining universal matters and specifying their scope. He clarifies that they encompass most existents, specifically the three main categories, and explains that topics like non-existence and necessity will be discussed incidentally.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition carefully. Notice that universal matters are said to encompass 'most existents', which he immediately specifies as the necessary, substance, and accident.
2
Identify the incidental topics
Notice that non-existence and impossibility are studied 'incidentally' (bi al-‘arad), and necessity is studied as a division of absolute necessity. Ask yourself why these are mentioned.
This sets boundaries for what will be discussed.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why the study of universal matters is the second purpose and what it contributes to the overall science of kalam.
The next lesson will explain its placement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المقصد الثاني: في الأمور العامة، وهو ما يعم أكثر الموجودات، الواجب، والجوهر، والعرض، فيكون البحث عن العدم والامتناع بالعرض وعن الوجوب لكونه من أقسام مطلق الوجوب وبيانها في فصول. قد سبقت الإشارة إلى أن وجه تقديم هذا المقصد على الأربعة الباقية قد توقف بعض بياناتها عليه، ووجه إفراده عنها مع كونه عائداً إليها، هو أنه لما كان البحث عن أحوال الموجود وقد انقسم إلى الواجب، والجوهر، والعرض واختفى[؟] كل منها بأحوال تعرف في بابه احتيج إلى باب المعرفة الأحوال المشتركة بين الثلاثة كالوجود والعدم والوحدة، أو الاثنين فقط، كالحدوث والكثرة، وبهذا يظهر أن المراد بالموجودات في قولهم الأمور العامة ما يعم أكثر الموجودات هو أقسامه الثلاثة التي هي الواجب، والجوهر، والعرض، لا أفراده التي لا سبيل للعقل إلى حصرها وتعيين الأكثر منها والحكم بأن مثل القلة والكثرة يعم أكثرها، ولا خفاء في أن المقصود بالنظر ما يتعلق به عرض علمي ويترتب عليه مقصود أصلي من الفن، ولا يكون له ذكر في أحد المقاصد بالاصالة، وإلا فكثير من الأمور الشاملة مما لا يبحث عنه في الباب كالكمية، والكيفية والإضافة، والمعلومية، والمقدورية، وسائر مباحث الكليات الخمس والحد والرسم والوضع، والحمل، بل عامة المقولات الثانية، ولا يضر كون البعض اعتبارياً محضاً، أو غير مختص بالموجود، لأن بعض ما يبحث عنه أيضاً كذلك كالإمكان
(He said): The second purpose: concerning universal matters, which is what encompasses most existents: the necessary, the substance, and the accident. So the discussion will be about non-existence and impossibility incidentally, and about necessity because it is a division of absolute necessity, and their explanation in chapters. Reference has already been made to the reason for advancing this purpose over the remaining four, because some of their explanations depend on it, and the reason for separating it from them despite it being a branch of them, is that since the investigation concerns the states of the existent and it has been divided into the necessary, the substance, and the accident, and each is distinguished [?] by states known in its own chapter, there was need for a chapter on the knowledge of the states common among the three, like existence and non-existence and unity, or only two, like temporality and multiplicity. By this it becomes clear that what is meant by 'existents' in their statement 'universal matters that encompass most existents' is its three divisions, which are the necessary, the substance, and the accident, not its individuals, which the intellect has no way to enumerate and determine the majority of them and judge that such as fewness and multitude encompass most of them. And it is not hidden that the intended object of consideration is what has a scientific aspect and yields an original purpose of the discipline, and which is not mentioned in any of the purposes primarily; otherwise, many universal matters would not be investigated in this chapter, like quantity, quality, relation, knownness, potenciness, and the rest of the investigations of the five universals, definition, description, positing, and predication, indeed all of the second categories. And it does not harm that some of them are merely mental or not specific to the existent, because some of what is investigated is also like that, such as possibility.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is both a definition and an explanation. The author begins by defining the second purpose (المقصد الثاني) of the work: 'universal matters' (الأمور العامة), which are those that encompass most existents—namely the necessary (الواجب), substance (الجوهر), and accident (العرض). He immediately notes that topics like non-existence (العدم) and impossibility (الامتناع) will be discussed only incidentally (بالعرض), and necessity (الوجوب) will be discussed as a division of absolute necessity. Then he explains why this purpose is placed before the remaining four: some of their explanations depend on it. He also explains why it is separated from them even though it returns to them: because the investigation is about the states of the existent, which is divided into three categories, each with its own specific states. There is a need for a chapter on shared states like existence, non-existence, unity, or those shared by two categories like temporality and multiplicity. From this, the author clarifies that 'most existents' refers to the three categories, not individual beings (أفراد), which the intellect cannot enumerate. He then restricts the investigation to topics that have a scientific aspect (عرض علمي) and yield an original purpose of the discipline. Many general matters are excluded, such as quantity, quality, relation, and so on, as well as all of the second categories. Finally, he notes that it does not harm if some of these are purely mental (اعتباري محض) or not specific to the existent, because some investigated topics, like possibility (الإمكان), are also like that.

Key Terms
الأمور العامة
الواجب
الجوهر
العرض

D) Individuals, substances, and accidents

Key Takeaway
Universal matters are the common states of the three categories of existent: the necessary, substance, and accident.

?
You are a compound of substance (your body) and accidents (your attributes). Recognizing that universal matters cover these categories, how does it change the way you view your daily struggles—are they eternal realities or passing accidents? What practical step can you take to anchor your identity in the Necessary Being rather than in changing circumstances?
?
If the universal matters are 'what encompasses most existents,' then your existence is part of that scope. What is one concrete change you can make in your life to align more with the purpose of your existence as a created being?

Why Universal Matters Are Discussed Here
Sanusi justifies the placement of universal matters and delimits their subject matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the discussion of universal matters is placed before the other purposes and what topics are excluded from it.

Core Idea
Universal matters are a necessary introduction to the other purposes because they deal with shared states.

Sanusi now explains the reasoning behind the placement of this purpose and clarifies exactly what is meant by 'most existents'. He also delineates which universal matters are investigated and which are left to other sciences. This passage is key to understanding the structure of the entire book.

Guided Path
1
Read the justification for placement
Read the part where Sanusi explains why this purpose is advanced before the other four. Notice that some of their explanations depend on it.
This shows the systematic nature of the work.
2
Understand the clarification of 'most existents'
Sanusi clarifies that 'most existents' refers to the three categories, not individuals. The intellect cannot enumerate individuals or judge which are more numerous.
This avoids a possible misunderstanding.
3
Identify what is excluded
Sanusi lists many general matters that are not investigated in this chapter, such as quantity, quality, etc. He also notes that some investigated topics are purely mental.
This defines the boundaries of the discussion.

Answering the Objection: Studying Non-Existents and Specific Attributes
Objection and answer regarding scope of universal matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi justifies the inclusion of non-existents and necessary attributes in the study of universal matters.

Core Idea
Non-existents and necessary attributes are studied indirectly (bi-l-'arad).

This passage presents an objection against studying non-existents (such as impossibility and non-existence) and attributes specific to the Necessary Being (such as necessity and eternity). The objector argues these do not belong in a discussion of universal matters that cover all existents. Al-Sanusi responds by clarifying that such topics are studied not in themselves, but incidentally, as opposites or as instances of broader universal concepts. This lesson unpacks that subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify what the objector claims: that studying impossibility and non-existence, and necessity and eternity, goes beyond the scope of universal matters because they do not cover all existents.
2
Study the author's answer
Notice how the author divides the answer: non-existence and impossibility are studied indirectly because they contrast with existence and possibility; necessity and eternity are studied as divisions of absolute necessity and absolute eternity.
The terms بالعرض (incidentally) and من جهة (from the perspective) are key.
3
Understand the philosophers' view on eternity
The author notes that the discussion of eternity is according to the philosophers who assert the eternity of abstract entities, motion, time, and other substances and accidents. This shows the context of the debate.
The passage ends abruptly; be aware that the full text may continue.
4
Reflect on the concept of 'bil-'arad'
Consider why studying something indirectly is still valid. The author argues that even though non-existence is not an existent, it is studied because it is the opposite of existence, a universal notion.
This is a common argument in logic and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد يبحث عما لا يشمل الموجود أصلاً كالامتناع والعدم وعما يخص الواجب قطعاً كالوجوب والقدم. قلنا : لما كان البحث مقصوراً على أحوال الموجود، كان بحث العدم والامتناع بالعرض لكونهما في مقابلة الوجود والإمكان وبحث الوجوب والقدم، من جهة كونهما من أقسام مطلق الوجوب والقدم، أعني ضرورة الوجود بالذات أو بالغير وعدم المسبوقية بالعدم وهما من الأمور الشاملة، أما الوجوب فظاهر، وأما القدم فعلى رأي الفلاسفة، حيث يقولون بقدم المجردات، والحركة، والزمان وغيرهم من الجواهر والأعراض، ونظر
For it is said: sometimes one investigates what does not encompass the existent at all, like impossibility and non-existence, and what is specific to the necessary absolutely, like necessity and eternity. We say: Since the investigation is confined to the states of the existent, the investigation of non-existence and impossibility is incidental because they are in opposition to existence and possibility; and the investigation of necessity and eternity is from the perspective that they are divisions of absolute necessity and eternity, i.e., necessity of existence by essence or by another, and non-precedence by non-existence, and they are among the universal matters. As for necessity, it is obvious; as for eternity, it is according to the opinion of the philosophers, since they assert the eternity of abstract entities, motion, time, and other substances and accidents, and consideration [?]
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (فإن قيل... قلنا). The objector points out that the study of universal matters should not include topics that are not general, such as impossibility (الامتناع) and non-existence (العدم), which do not encompass any existent, nor topics specific to the necessary being (الواجب) such as necessity (الوجوب) and eternity (القدم). The author answers by explaining that these are studied indirectly (بالعرض). Non-existence and impossibility are studied because they are the opposites of existence and possibility, which are universal. Necessity and eternity are studied as divisions of absolute necessity and absolute eternity: necessity means necessary existence by essence or by another, and eternity means not being preceded by non-existence. These are universal concepts. The author notes that the study of necessity is obvious, but the study of eternity is according to the philosophers' opinion who assert the eternity of abstract entities (المجردات), motion (الحركة), time (الزمان), and other substances and accidents. The passage ends abruptly with 'ونظر' (and consideration), possibly indicating a continuation or a concluding remark.

Key Terms

العدم
بالعرض
الوجوب
القدم
المجردات
الحركة
الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that studying non-existence or necessary attributes is irrelevant to the study of universal matters because they are not common to all existents.
Correction: The author shows they are studied indirectly (by opposition) or as instances of broader universal concepts, thus still falling under the scope.

Checkpoint

A) They are existent in the mind
B) They are studied indirectly (بالعرض) because they contrast with existence and possibility
C) They are also existent in some way
D) They are necessary for the proof of God

Key Takeaway
Even topics that do not directly cover all existents can be studied in kalam when they are incidental to universal concepts or are instances of broader categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological topic that seems negative or about what does not exist (like non-existence or failure), do you tend to dismiss it as irrelevant? How might approaching it 'incidentally'—as a contrast to something positive—give you deeper insight into the positive?

This passage shows that even Allah's specific attributes (like necessity and eternity) can be studied through broader categories. Does this challenge any assumptions you have about the uniqueness of these attributes? In what way does it help you appreciate both God's transcendence and the usefulness of universal concepts?

Chapter Outline: Existence and Non-existence
Chapter outline listing six investigations on existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six topics that will be discussed in the first chapter on existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Six investigations unpack the single concept of existence and non-existence.

This lesson presents the roadmap for the first chapter, which is dedicated to the foundational concepts of existence and non-existence. The author lists six distinct investigations that will be explored in detail. Before diving into the arguments, it is helpful to see the overall plan.

Guided Path
1
Read the outline
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a simple list of six topics.
This is just the table of contents; each item will be expanded later.
2
Identify the logic of the sequence
The topics move from the basic conception to more specific aspects like divisions and predication. Think about why they are ordered this way.
3
Reflect on the significance
Understanding the outline helps you anticipate the arguments. Ask yourself: why start with self-evident nature?
This connects to the overall methodology of the work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في الوجود والعدم وفيه سنة مباحث:
١ - في تصوره وأنه بديهي.
٢ - في أنه مشترك بين الموجودات زائد على الذات.
٣ - في تقسيمه إلى عيني وذهني وخطي وما يتعلق بذلك.
٤ - في أنه يرادف الثبوت والعدم.
٥ - في تمايز الإعدام في العقل.
٦ - في أن كلاً من الوجود والعدم يقع محمولاً ورابطه.
The first chapter: On existence and non-existence. It contains six investigations:
1. On its conception and that it is self-evident.
2. On that it is common among existents and additional to the essence.
3. On its division into external, mental, and verbal and what pertains to that.
4. On that it is synonymous with affirmation and non-existence.
5. On the distinction of privations in the intellect.
6. On that each of existence and non-existence occurs as a predicate and its copula.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the table of contents for the first chapter of the work, which is devoted to the concepts of existence and non-existence. The author lists six subsections (mabāḥith) that will be treated. The first investigates the conception of existence and non-existence and asserts that it is self-evident (badīhī). The second discusses whether existence is a concept common to all existents and whether it is something additional to the essence (zāʾid ʿalā al-dhāt). The third divides existence into external (ʿaynī), mental (dhihnī), and verbal (lafẓī) and related matters. The fourth examines whether existence is synonymous with affirmation (thubūt) and non-existence with negation. The fifth discusses how privations (al-ʿadam) are distinguished in the mind. The sixth addresses how both existence and non-existence function as predicates and their copula (rābiṭ). This outline sets the stage for the detailed discussions that follow.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم
بديهي
مشترك
زائد على الذات
عيني
ذهني
خطي
ثبوت
تمايز الإعدام
محمول
رابط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage contains the full discussions of each topic.
Correction: It is merely an outline; each topic will be elaborated in its own section.

Checkpoint

A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The first chapter will systematically examine existence and non-existence across six specific investigations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Six Investigations on Existence and Non-existence
Author lists the six investigations of the first chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six investigations that structure the first chapter on existence and non-existence in kalam, and how they form a coherent inquiry.

Core Idea
Six investigations provide a complete framework for analyzing existence and non-existence.

This passage presents the roadmap for the entire first chapter. Before diving into detailed arguments, al-Taftazani outlines six key investigations that will be pursued. This orientation helps the student see the logical flow and the major questions addressed. The six topics range from basic conception to the use of existence and non-existence as predicates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the six investigations
Carefully read the Arabic and the translation. Write down each of the six mabahith (investigations) in your own words.
Notice that each investigation is introduced with a number.
2
Understand key terms
Familiarize yourself with terms like wujud, 'adam, badihi, za'id 'ala al-dhat, 'ayni, dhihni, khatti, thubut, and rabita. These will recur throughout the chapter.
Use the key_terms list for reference.
3
Consider the logical progression
Why does the author start with self-evidence, then move to commonality, then division, then synonymy, then differentiation, then predicate usage? Think about how each step builds on the previous.
The progression moves from simple conception to complex logical roles.
4
Reflect on the coherence of the six topics
Though they seem separate, these six collectively exhaust the major issues about existence and non-existence. How do they complement each other?
Avoid the misconception that they are unrelated.

الفصل الأول في الوجود والعدم وفيه سنة مباحث
١ - في تصوره وأنه بديهي
٢ - في أنه مشترك بين الموجودات زائد على الذات
٣ - في تقسيمه إلى عيني وذهني وخطي وما يتعلق بذلك
٤ - في أنه يرادف الثبوت والعدم
٥ - في تمايز الإعدام في العقل

The first chapter: On existence and non-existence, and it contains six investigations: 1 - On their conception and that it is self-evident; 2 - On that they are common among existents and additional to the essence; 3 - On their division into concrete, mental, and verbal existence and what pertains to that; 4 - On that they are synonymous with permanence (thubut) and non-existence; 5 - On the differentiation of non-existents in the mind; 6 - On that each of existence and non-existence occurs as predicate and copula.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and introduction listing the six investigations (mabahith) of the first chapter on wujud (existence) and 'adam (non-existence). The author, al-Taftazani, begins by stating the chapter's subject matter. He then enumerates six topics: the first investigates whether the concepts of existence and non-existence are self-evident (badihi) or acquired. The second explores whether existence is a common attribute added to the essence (za'id 'ala al-dhat) of existents. The third divides existence into 'ayni (concrete, external), dhihni (mental), and khatti (verbal) and related issues. The fourth examines synonymy between wujud and thubut (permanence) and between 'adam and 'adam. The fifth asks whether non-existents (i'dam) are distinct in the mind. The sixth addresses whether both terms can serve as predicates (mahmul) and as copula (rabita) in propositions. This outline sets the agenda for detailed discussion in the chapter.

The Four Levels of Existence
Sanusi enumerates the four levels of existence and the ordering of indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four levels of existence (external, mental, verbal, written) and how each subsequent level indicates the preceding one, establishing a hierarchy.

Core Idea
Existence flows from external reality through mind, speech, and script—each level points back to the one before.

This passage lays the foundation for understanding how theological knowledge is transmitted through multiple layers of reality. Sanusi identifies four distinct levels of existence—from the thing itself to its inscription—and explains the relationship between them. This hierarchy is crucial for grasping how indication works in later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: external (عيني), mental (ذهني), verbal (لفظي), written (خطي).
2
Identify the four levels and their order
The passage lists four levels: external existence, mental existence, verbal existence, written existence. Notice the order: external is first, then mental, then verbal, then written.
The passage also mentions that verbal and written existence are metaphorical when attributed to the thing's essence.
3
Understand the indication chain
Each later level indicates the one before: mental indicates external, verbal indicates mental, written indicates verbal. This chain is the basis for the three indications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخارج لكانت ذلك الشيء، كما أن الشجر لو تجسم لكان ذلك الشجر، ثم الوجود في العبارة ثم في الكتابة وهما من حيث الإضافة إلى ذات الشيء وحقيقته مجازيان، لأن الموجود من زيد في اللفظ صوت موضوع بإزائه، وفي الخط نقش موضوع بإزائه اللفظ الدال عليه، لا ذات زيد ولا صورته . نعم إذا أضيف إلى اللفظ الموضوع بإزائه أو النقش الموضوع بإزاء ذلك اللفظ كان وجوداً حقيقياً، من قبيل الوجود في الأعيان، ولكل لاحق فيما ذكرنا من الترتيب دلالة على السابق فللذهني على العيني، وللفظي على الذهني، وللخطي على اللفظي، فتحقق ثلاث دلالات أولاها: عقلية محضة : لا يختلف فيها بحسب اختلاف الأشخاص والأوضاع الدال ولا المدلول، إذا بأي لفظ عبر عن السماء . فالموجود منها في الخارج هو الشخص وفي الذهن هو الصورة المعينة المطابقة له ، والأخريان أعني دلالة اللفظ على الصورة الذهنية ودلالة الخط على اللفظ وضعيتان يختلف في الأولى منهما الدال بأن تعين طائفة لفظاً كالسماء ، وطائفة أخرى لفظاً آخر كما في الفارسية وغيرها لا المدلول لأن الصورة الذهنية لا تختلف باختلاف اللغات وتختلف في الثانية: أعني دلالة الخط على اللفظ الدال والمدلول جميعاً، واختلاف الدال لا يختص بحالة اختلاف المدلول، بل قد يكون مع كلفظ السماء، يكتب بصور مختلفة بحسب اختلاف الاصطلاحات في الكتابة .
External [existence] would be that thing, just as if the tree became embodied it would be that tree. Then existence in expression and then in writing, and both are metaphorical in relation to the essence and reality of the thing, because the existent of Zayd in speech is a sound placed in correspondence to him, and in script it is an inscription placed in correspondence to the speech that indicates him, not Zayd's essence nor his image. Yes, if it is attributed to the speech placed in correspondence to him or the inscription placed in correspondence to that speech, it becomes real existence, of the kind of existence in external entities. And for each subsequent in the order we mentioned there is an indication of the preceding: for the mental of the external, for the verbal of the mental, and for the written of the verbal. Thus three indications are realized: the first of them is purely rational, not differing according to differences of persons or conventions, neither the indicator nor the indicated; for no matter which word is used for 'sky', the existent of it externally is the individual, and in the mind is the specific image corresponding to it. The other two—namely, the indication of the speech to the mental image and the indication of the script to the speech—are conventional. In the first of them, the indicator varies in that one group assigns a word like 'sky' and another group assigns another word as in Persian and others, but not the indicated, because the mental image does not differ with different languages. And it varies in the second, namely the indication of the script to the speech: both the indicator and the indicated vary, and the variation of the indicator is not restricted to a case of variation of the indicated; rather it may be that with the same word 'sky', it is written in different forms according to differences of conventions in writing.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage explains the four levels of existence—external (a'yani), mental (dhihni), verbal (lafzi), and written (khatti)—and the three types of indication (dalala) linking them. The author begins by noting that existence in expression and writing is metaphorical when attributed to the thing’s essence; only when attributed to the corresponding word or inscription does it become real. Then he states that each subsequent level in this order indicates the preceding one: the mental indicates the external, the verbal indicates the mental, and the written indicates the verbal. This yields three indications. The first (mental to external) is purely rational (aqli mahda), unaffected by differences in persons or languages—the mental image of the sky is the same regardless of the word used. The other two (verbal to mental, written to verbal) are conventional (wad'iyyan). The verbal indicator varies by language, but the mental image (the indicated) does not. The written indicator varies both in the script forms and in the words they represent, as the same word may be written differently across conventions.

Key Terms
الوجود
العيني
الذهني
اللفظي
الخطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verbal and written existence are real existence of the thing itself.
Correction: They are metaphorical when attributed to the thing's essence, but real when attributed to the word or inscription.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is existence in speech considered real existence?
A) When attributed to Zayd's essence
B) When attributed to the sound placed in correspondence to Zayd
C) When it is the same as external existence
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy of existence (external, mental, verbal, written) shows that each later level points back to the earlier, with verbal and written existence being real only in relation to their immediate referents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah or His attributes, do you ever mistake your words for the reality? How can you ensure your heart is present with the meaning, not just the sound?
?
The written text of this lesson is a script that points to words that point to concepts that point to reality. As you read, are you content with the words, or are you striving to reach the reality they point to?
?
In your daily life, do you treat mental constructs (like opinions, labels) as if they were the things themselves? When was the last time you caught yourself confusing a mental image for truth?

Three Types of Indication: Rational and Conventional
Sanusi classifies the three indications and explains their rational vs conventional nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of indication—one rational and two conventional—and how they differ in terms of variation of indicator and indicated.

Core Idea
The first indication (mental→external) is universal and rational; the other two are language-dependent and conventional.

Having established the four levels of existence, Sanusi now examines how these levels are connected through three types of indication. He distinguishes a purely rational indication—the mental image directly pointing to the external object—from two conventional indications that involve language and writing. This distinction is essential for understanding how knowledge is communicated across different languages and scripts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three indications
The passage lists three indications: mental indicating external, verbal indicating mental, written indicating verbal. Understand each.
2
Classify them as rational or conventional
The first (mental→external) is purely rational (عقلية محضة). The other two are conventional (وضعيتان). Notice the criteria: does the indication vary with language or script?
3
Analyze variations in conventional indications
For the verbal→mental indication, the indicator (the word) varies by language, but the indicated (mental image) does not. For the written→verbal indication, both the indicator (script) and the indicated (the word) can vary.
The author gives the example of the word 'sky' being written in different script forms.

Source Text
